Pro Private Markets
Can Computers Outperform Deal-Makers?

374 words
31 March 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Can Computers Outperform Deal-Makers?

BlackRock Inc. is showing some stock pickers the door.

The world's largest asset manager concluded it had to do more to keep up with highly competitive algorithmic and other computerized trading, which has revolutionized the financial industry. In place of human asset managers who have created its trading strategies, BlackRock is putting computer models to work.

Many investing professionals of all stripes have struggled with similar concerns weighing on BlackRock. In today's hyperdriven financial industry, everyone is looking for an edge, and inevitably that draws them ever closer to data, analytics and, perhaps most dramatically, automation.

So why shouldn't venture capitalists follow suit?

Some already are trying it out. At Correlation Ventures, the managers say they have delegated the heavy lifting to computer models.

The co-investing firm, which closed its first fund in 2012, aims to achieve solid returns and mitigate risk by making dozens of investments each year across all sectors and investment stages. Its data-heavy approach relies almost entirely on proprietary analytics and predictive modeling to glean positive or negative investment signals, says Managing Director David Coats.

Mr. Coats says it's still a little early for his firm, which closed on $200 million for its second fund, to say how well the approach works. But he is confident its "empirical, objective" method enables Correlation to make quick decisions about writing checks, and lots of them, which are confined to co-investing. The first fund, with 121 portfolio companies, has had at least five exits.

Automation by definition takes the human out of the process. So is Sand Hill Road about to look like a ghost town? Even Mr. Coats hopes not. He says that for lead investors, "internal" human judgment is irreplaceable. But predictive modeling matters.

"If our thesis holds," says Mr. Coats, "our portfolio will be tilted to the more favorable outcomes."

Davis's Take: Can Computers Outperform Deal-Makers? As stock pickers turn to automation, some venture capitalists write checks with predictive models

By Alexander Davis 
349 words
31 March 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

BlackRock Inc. is showing some stock pickers the door.

The world's largest asset manager concluded it had to do more to keep up with highly competitive algorithmic and other computerized trading, which has revolutionized the financial industry. In place of human asset managers who have created its trading strategies, BlackRock is putting computer models to work.

The co-investing firm, which closed its first fund in 2012, aims to achieve solid returns and mitigate risk by making dozens of investments each year across all sectors and investment stages. Wealth managers play  robo advisers  at their own game

Richard Henderson 
757 words
30 March 2017
07:24
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Financial planners are fighting back against the rise of  robo advisers  by arming themselves with the weaponry of their automated foes.

Robo advisers   digital platforms that use artificial intelligence to provide low-cost online investment services   are tipped to take business from traditional wealth managers. But human advisers are using the same technologies that underpin such platforms to provide clients with more tailored advice and communication, which wealthy investors are willing to pay for.

Tools that help manage interactions with clients   known as customer relationship management (CRM) systems   will soon be able to analyse data such as a client s social media activity to work out their investment goals, says Gauthier Vincent, head of Deloitte s US wealth management consulting business.

 Advisers are thinking,  There s a lot of info out there I would love to have to create rich profiles of prospects so I can increase the odds of success when I [contact] them,   he says.

Technology is also helping advisers streamline estate planning and compliance, as well as shaping how, when and what they communicate with their clients. For instance, advisers plan to use technology to send tailored messages with portfolio details and projections to calm clients during periods of market volatility.

 For an adviser to call 100 clients, that will take a week or two,  Mr Vincent says, but these emerging tools allow advisers to  work with clients [in a way] that was previously unheard of. 

Some wealth companies have high expectations for what they can achieve with the new technology and are setting aggressive goals. Tej Vakta, head of wealth management services at Capgemini, a consultant and technology provider, says one bank he works with wants to increase the ratio of clients to each of its advisers from 55 to around 350. The bank is trying to achieve this by targeting clients with around $250,000 in assets whom it believes will become millionaires within 10 years.

 Banks want to go after these [clients] but have a limited workforce [and are asking] what are the different things they can do with machine learning, with automation, with large virtual advice,  Mr Vakta says.

Many advisers fear they will be left behind as more complex tools develop and clients demand tech-savvy service. Ensuring that technology tools are efficient is one of the top three challenges cited this year by FT 400 survey respondents. Those who are able to blend technology with a human touch stand to benefit: a 2015 survey by Fidelity, the asset manager, found that advisers who made greater use of technology had significantly more assets than competitors.

But how these tools are used by advisers will be critical to improving the client experience, says Ben Marks, chief investment officer at Marks Group Wealth Management, and an FT 400 adviser.

The 12 people in his practice   which oversees $800m in assets   note all client interactions in its CRM system, which can be used for data analysis. Staff also discuss clients in team meetings so everyone is up to speed and can deal with inquiries from clients directly.  From a client experience, that helps,  Mr Marks says.  It makes them feel special. 

The company recently began using financial planning technology, but the system alone is not enough to meet clients  needs. Mr Marks had to hire a specialist to input and analyse the relevant data.  There still has to be a human being involved, so I wouldn t say it necessarily saves us time, but it certainly adds an incredible amount of value,  he says.

Mr Marks  company is using a combination of technology with a human touch to communicate with clients, too.  You can use technology to help distribute information and formulate your recommendations, but I don t think you re going to replicate that human element,  he says.  People want and pay for advice no matter what generation they re from. 

National brokerages that offer a range of investment services, colloquially known as wirehouses, are also increasing their use of technology to gain better access to new and existing clients. This month Merrill Lynch, a wirehouse with around 16,000 advisers, updated its web portal to give clients easier access to services. Over the past few years the company has developed technology that monitors clients  investments and liabilities.

 The technology has allowed data analysis to come to be extremely extensive and comprehensive, not only internally, but as we present it to each client,  says Kent Pearce, a Merrill Lynch adviser.

FT REPORT - FT 400: TOP FINANCIAL ADVISERS
Wealth managers play 'robo advisers' at own game; Technology

By Richard Henderson 
777 words
30 March 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FIV1
02
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Automation and big data mining are helping planners compete, says Richard Henderson

Financial planners are fighting back against the rise of "robo advisers" by arming themselves with the weaponry of their automated foes.

Robo advisers - digital platforms that use artificial intelligence to provide low-cost online investment services - are tipped to take business from traditional wealth managers. But human advisers are using the same technologies that underpin such platforms to provide clients with more tailored advice and communication, which wealthy investors are willing to pay for.

Tools that help manage interactions with clients- known as customer relationship management (CRM) systems - will soon be able to analyse data such as a client's social media activity to work out their investment goals, says Gauthier Vincent, head of Deloitte's US wealth management consulting business.

"Advisers are thinking, 'There's a lot of info out there I would love to have to create rich profiles of prospects so I can increase the odds of success when I [contact] them,'" he says.

Technology is also helping advisers streamline estate planning and compliance, as well as shaping how, when and what they communicate with their clients. For instance, advisers plan to use technology to send tailored messages with portfolio details and projections to calm clients during periods of market volatility.

"For an adviser to call 100 clients, that will take a week or two," Mr Vincent says, but these emerging tools allow advisers to "work with clients [in a way] that was previously unheard of."

Some wealth companies have high expectations for what they can achieve with the new technology and are setting aggressive goals. Tej Vakta, head of wealth management services at Capgemini, a consultant and technology provider, says one bank he works with wants to increase the ratio of clients to each of its advisers from 55 to around 350. The bank is trying to achieve this by targeting clients with around $250,000 in assets whom it believes will become millionaires within 10 years.

"Banks want to go after these [clients] but have a limited workforce [and are asking] what are the different things they can do with machine learning, with automation, with large virtual advice," Mr Vakta says.

Many advisers fear they will be left behind as more complex tools develop and clients demand tech-savvy service. Ensuring that technology tools are efficient is one of the top three challenges cited this year by FT 400 survey respondents. Those who are able to blend technology with a human touch stand to benefit: a 2015 survey by Fidelity , the asset manager, found that advisers who made greater use of technology had significantly more assets than competitors.

But how these tools are used by advisers will be critical to improving the client experience, says Ben Marks, chief investment officer at Marks Group Wealth Management, and an FT 400 adviser.

The 12 people in his practice - which oversees $800m in assets - note all client interactions in its CRM system, which can be used for data analysis. Staff also discuss clients in team meetings so everyone is up to speed and can deal with inquiries from clients directly. "From a client experience, that helps," Mr Marks says. "It makes them feel special."

The company recently began using financial planning technology, but the system alone is not enough to meet clients' needs. Mr Marks had to hire a specialist to input and analyse the relevant data. "There still has to be a human being involved, so I wouldn't say it necessarily saves us time, but it certainly adds an incredible amount of value," he says.

Mr Marks' company is using a combination of technology with a human touch to communicate with clients, too. "You can use technology to help distribute information and formulate your recommendations, but I don't think you're going to replicate that human element," he says. "People want and pay for advice no matter what generation they're from."

National brokerages that offer a range of investment services, colloquially known as wirehouses, are also increasing their use of technology to gain better access to new and existing clients. This month Merrill Lynch, a wirehouse with around 16,000 advisers, updated its web portal to give clients easier access to services. Over the past few years the company has developed technology that monitors clients' investments and liabilities.

"The technology has allowed data analysis to come to be extremely extensive and comprehensive, not only internally, but as we present it to each client," says Kent Pearce, a Merrill Lynch adviser.

Short Sellers Pounce on Sears Landlord; Short interest on Seritage Growth Properties hit $368.5 million Wednesday

By Esther Fung 
375 words
30 March 2017
23:21
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Short sellers are betting against Seritage Growth Properties, the real-estate investment trust spun off by Sears Holdings Corp. in 2015 that holds the struggling retailer's property assets.

The so-called short interest, a measure of negative bets, on Seritage hit an all-time high of $368.5 million on Wednesday, according to S3 Partners, a financial analytics firm.

In 2016 and 2015, average daily short interest totaled $203.1 million and $80.7 million, respectively.

"One might look at Seritage Growth Properties as an alternative short play to indirectly gain exposure to Sears' demise," said Matthew Unterman, a director at S3 Partners.

In a short sale, an investor borrows shares of a company and sells them, in hopes of buying the shares later, at a lower price, to repay the loan and pocket the difference. Short interest tracks the number of shares that have been sold short but not repaid.

About 8.65 million Seritage shares have been sold short, or roughly 35% of the company's publicly traded shares.

Seritage declined to comment.

In a securities filing last week, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sears-stock-stumbles-after-going-concern-warning-1490191329" after seven years of losses. Sears' Chief Financial Officer Jason Hollar later sought to assuage investors by writing in a blog post that the retailer is "a viable business that can meet its financial and other obligations for the foreseeable future."

In early January, the retailer said it was shutting 150 stores across the country and "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sears-sells-craftsman-brand-to-stanley-black-decker-1483623215".

Some investors expect shopping-center landlords, including Seritage, to struggle with a faster-than-expected pace of store closures.

In 2015, Sears Holdings"https://www.wsj.com/articles/seritage-raises-1-6-billion-to-begin-trading-after-spinoff-from-sears-1436185521" for $2.72 billion. Of these, 220 properties are leased to Sears Holdings and house Sears or Kmart stores.

Seritage has been diversifying its revenue stream in recent quarters. The New York-based REIT said it reduced its exposure to rental income from Sears Holdings to 64% at the end of 2016 from 80% when the REIT was formed.

Pro Private Markets
Martin's Take: RBC Pitches Silicon Valley on Canada; A cradle of machine learning  ingenuity wants its talent back

By Scott Martin 
365 words
30 March 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Canada wants its machine learning talent back.

The Royal Bank of Canada sent its electronic trading chiefs to Silicon Valley on Wednesday to tout the bank's laser focus on machine learning. It was a pitch about all of the big problems in machine learning that RBC wants to solve. That's important because everyone knows techies need a big mission, especially if there's no FB stock involved.

"The compliance budget is massive, and these are not optional spends," said Stephen Bain, managing director of institutional equity trading at RBC, speaking of the need for regulatory-focused tech projects.

RBC's trading business needs leaps in machine learning to improve trading recommendations, sleuth out indicators of interest, anomaly detection, trading surveillance and risk aggregation reporting. Needless to say, it must also stay one step ahead of high-frequency traders seeking to exploit split-second information, asymmetry discovery and algorithmic advantages.

RBC's Shary Mudassir, director of global algorithmic trading, explained the bank had about a dozen algo spots unfilled but was seeking certain "unicorn" employees. These people had to deeply understand trading, algorithms and have people skills.

"There's an opportunity for machine learning to tell when and where you deploy certain tactics," said Mr. Bain, citing an ongoing war between HFT trading shops outside and big banks, popularized in Michael Lewis's book "Flash Boys."

Canada's University of Waterloo, of course, is a hotbed of machine learning experts, now part of an exodus of talent heading to the Valley to start companies.

As a lure back, Mr. Bain pointed out that people who made $150,000 to $200,000 earned just a basic livable wage in Silicon Valley and that property prices are far higher there than in Canada, where quality of life is better.

Polite as the Canadians were, they didn't mention an easy sell on leaving the Valley for Canada: the Trump factor.

And it doesn't take an algorithm to figure that out.

Pro Private Markets
Lim's Take: North Carolina Pension Brandishes Ax on Funds in Cost-Cutting Push; Pension explores sales of stakes in funds it has cooled on

618 words
30 March 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Live Video Event: The Artificial Intelligence-Biotech Convergence

Artificial intelligence startups are bringing their advances to biotechnology, betting that intelligent machines will revolutionize disease detection and drug discovery. Join WSJ Pro Venture Capital today at 2 p.m. Eastern for an interview with Gini Deshpande, founder and chief executive of biotechnology company NuMedii, and Bradford Cross, a partner at venture-capital firm Data Collective, on the potential of AI in biotech. Viewers' questions will be answered.

North Carolina Pension Brandishes Ax on Funds in Cost-Cutting Push

The North Carolina Retirement Systems wants to cut ties with several money managers and write bigger checks to fewer funds, in a push to slash its investment fees.

The $92 billion state pension fund is working with investment bank Houlihan Lokey Inc. to explore a sale of some stakes in real-estate and private-equity funds it has cooled on, according to a pension presentation. It is looking at ways to exit or restructure its fund-of-funds investments, a section of the market that faces mounting pressure to justify the extra layer of fees charged to pensions and endowments.

North Carolina's cost-saving drive on the heels of a campaign promise by North Carolina's new state treasurer, Dale Folwell, to reduce fees illustrates pressure among public funds to deploy money more cheaply as returns are squeezed by low interest rates. The pension is aiming to reap $100 million in cost savings from its investment program in the next four years.

As part of the effort, North Carolina plans to write larger checks to fewer funds across asset classes. It wants to invest in real estate, natural resources and other private markets through more separate accounts structured with special fees and terms, instead of parking its money in traditional funds.

The pension fund paid higher fees to money managers in real estate and private equity compared with what it paid to those that handled its money in other asset classes for the year ended June 30.

North Carolina paid outside real-estate managers an aggregate $196.1 million in fees and carried interest to oversee $8.35 billion in holdings. Private equity was the second-most-expensive asset class after real estate when external manager fees were calculated against portfolio holdings: North Carolina paid private-equity managers $92.9 million in fees and carry to oversee $4.38 billion in investments.

With pensions gaining more visibility into the fees and expenses they bear, they will be forced to weigh the trade-offs of investment shifts more closely.

North Carolina plans to push more money to indexed funds after a lukewarm showing by several active stock managers, according to the pension presentation. Direct lending funds as well as cheaper high-yield bond managers are slated to win more of the pension's money after a "disappointing performance" by some of its credit hedge funds.

As the state's cost-cutting drive continues, new winners and losers will surely emerge down the road.

Write to Dawn Lim at dawn.lim@wsj.com

The Property Report
Amazon a Force in U.K. Warehouses

By Art Patnaude 
746 words
29 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The warehouse property market in Europe just wouldn't be the same without Amazon.com Inc.

Amazon has become a major force in a logistics real-estate sector that is being reshaped by online shopping, according to investors and property brokers. In the U.K. alone, the U.S. online retail giant accounted for nearly a quarter of all warehouse property space leased last year, according to property broker Savills.

Once a dull corner of the commercial real-estate sector, warehouses have become vital for businesses offering same-day deliveries. E-commerce requires efficient networks linking massive distribution centers to smaller properties in towns and cities, a system often likened to a wheel with spokes.

Demand for space hit a record last year in Europe's biggest markets.

In all, 230 million square feet of logistics space was leased in the region, up from 205 million square feet in 2015, according to property broker CBRE.

As Amazon and other e-commerce companies pile into warehouses, investors increasingly are rushing to buy the properties.

While overall commercial real-estate investment volumes fell in Europe last year, industrial and logistics volumes hit a record 25 billion euros ($27.2 billion), up from 23.3 billion euros in 2015, according to CBRE.

"E-commerce is forcing investors to look at logistics and warehousing completely differently," said Andrew Jones, chief executive at LondonMetric Property PLC, a U.K. real-estate investment trust.

In Europe, online retailing has taken hold most strongly in the U.K., where last year, internet purchases accounted for 15% of retail purchases, according to data firm GlobalData. Germany followed at 9%, the data show.

Amazon, which dominates online shopping around the world, accounted for 27% of all U.K. online sales last year, according to consumer-market research firm Euromonitor. In Germany, Amazon's market share was even bigger, accounting for 40% of internet shopping sales, the data show.

Amazon leased 18 units last year in the U.K., 11% of the total number of units leased, according to Savills, but the 8 million square feet accounted for more than 23% of the total space leased.

The level "is largely unprecedented," said Kevin Mofid, a Savills research analyst.

In recent months, Amazon has said it would open several fulfillment centers across the region, including in France, Germany, Slovakia and Poland.

Amazon might not be as dominant in the U.K. this year after an exceptional 2016, brokers said, but it has continued to scoop up space. LondonMetric said last week it had leased warehouse space to Amazon.

LondonMetric aims to increase its logistics property holdings to 80% from about 60%, with a particular focus on properties in urban areas, Mr. Jones said. The company last week raised GBP 95.5 million ($119.9 million) through a share placing to fund such acquisitions.

---

'Last Mile' Crucial

To Same-Day Service

Warehouses near cities are known as the "last mile" of a delivery network, which has become increasingly important to firms such as Amazon.com Inc. offering same-day services.

Blackstone Group LP, among the most active investors in European warehouses, has picked up on the trend. The U.S. private-equity firm last week agreed to buy, alongside U.K.-based real-estate group M7 Real Estate, a portfolio of last-mile warehouses in Germany and the Netherlands for 1.28 billion euros, according to Hansteen Holdings, the seller.

Blackstone also is selling its expansive European warehouse arm, Logicor. The private-equity firm is marketing the sale at 13 billion euros, while simultaneously working on an initial public offering. While the sale is Blackstone's preferred route, experts said, either path would result in one of the biggest real-estate transactions ever in Europe.

In one of the biggest European real-estate deals last year, GIC Pte. Ltd., Singapore's sovereign-wealth fund, bought warehouse-property company P3 Logistic Parks for 2.4 billion euros.

Strong investor demand has sent yields sharply lower. The average capitalization rate, a measure of yield, on European industrial properties was 6.4% in the fourth quarter last year, steadily falling from 7.8% three years earlier, according to Real Capital Analytics.

"E-commerce has changed our markets globally," said Jack Cox, head of industrial and logistics capital markets at property broker CBRE. "And it's only just starting."

-- Art Patnaude

SoftBank Weighs New Rides Deal --- Company considers $6 billion investment to help China's Didi make self-driving push

By Kane Wu and Mayumi Negishi 
449 words
29 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SoftBank Group Corp. has approached Didi Chuxing Technology Co. of China about investing roughly $6 billion to help the ride-hailing firm expand in self-driving-car technologies, two people familiar with the matter said.

The bulk of the investment would likely come from SoftBank's planned $100 billion Vision Fund, in hopes of unlocking new value in China's expanding ride-hailing market, one of the people said. The technology fund, originally slated for a launch in January, has been delayed as SoftBank considers different demands from investors such as Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund, Abu Dhabi's Mubadala Development Co., Apple Inc., Qualcomm Inc. and Oracle Corp., according to another person close to the fund.

Didi became China's ride-hailing champion after it merged with rival Uber Technologies Inc.'s China operations last year. SoftBank -- like Tencent Holdings Ltd., Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and Baidu Inc. -- has been hunting for stakes in car-hailing apps. The Japanese group led a $750 million fundraising push last year in Singapore-based Grab, which is locked in a price war in Southeast Asia. Grab also counts Didi as an investor. SoftBank also led a $600 million financing round in Kuaidi Dache in 2015, which later merged with Didi.

Through the Vision Fund, SoftBank Chief Executive Masayoshi Son hopes to target multimillion-dollar investments in areas such as autonomous driving and artificial intelligence.

Since announcing the fund, SoftBank has invested in shared-office-space firm WeWork Cos. and satellite operator Intelsat SA, with plans to have the Vision Fund take stakes in the companies as well. It also plans to sell a stake in its U.K. semiconductor designer ARM Holdings PLC to the fund, people familiar with the matter have said.

Didi was valued at roughly $36 billion after the Uber China merger. As part of the deal, Uber also became the largest shareholder in Didi. Didi counts all three of China's largest technology companies -- Alibaba, Tencent and Baidu -- as shareholders.

A SoftBank investment could give Didi a boost when the company is facing hurdles in China. Several cities issued proposed industry regulations late last year that would restrict the types of vehicles that can be used and who can drive them. Meanwhile, the country's antitrust regulators opened an investigation on Didi's deal to acquire U.S.-based Uber's China unit.It isn't clear whether the investigation has concluded.

SoftBank's possible investment in Didi was reported earlier by Bloomberg.

The warehouse property market in Europe just wouldn't be the same without Amazon.com Inc.

Amazon has become a major force in a logistics real-estate sector that is being reshaped by online shopping, according to investors and property brokers. In the U.K. alone, the U.S. online retail giant accounted for nearly a quarter of all warehouse property space leased last year, according to property broker Savills.

Once a dull corner of the commercial real-estate sector, warehouses have become vital for businesses offering same-day deliveries. E-commerce requires efficient networks linking massive distribution centers to smaller properties in towns and cities, a system often likened to a wheel with spokes.

Demand for space hit a record last year in Europe's biggest markets. In all, 230 million square feet of logistics space was leased in the region, up from 205 million square feet in 2015, according to property broker CBRE.

As Amazon and other e-commerce companies pile into warehouses, investors increasingly are rushing to buy the properties. While overall commercial real-estate investment volumes fell in Europe last year, industrial and logistics volumes hit a record 25 billion euros ($27.2 billion), up from 23.3 billion euros in 2015, according to CBRE.

"E-commerce is forcing investors to look at logistics and warehousing completely differently," said Andrew Jones, chief executive at LondonMetric Property PLC, a U.K. real-estate investment trust.

In Europe, online retailing has taken hold most strongly in the U.K., where last year, internet purchases accounted for 15% of retail purchases, according to data firm GlobalData. Germany followed at 9%, the data show.

Amazon, which dominates online shopping around the world, accounted for 27% of all U.K. online sales last year, according to consumer-market research firm Euromonitor. In Germany, Amazon's market share was even bigger, accounting for 40% of internet shopping sales, the data show.

Amazon leased 18 units last year in the U.K., 11% of the total number of units leased, according to Savills, but the 8 million square feet accounted for more than 23% of the total space leased.

The level "is largely unprecedented," said Kevin Mofid, a Savills research analyst.

In recent months, Amazon has said it would open several fulfillment centers across the region, including in France, Germany, Slovakia and Poland.

Amazon might not be as dominant in the U.K. this year after an exceptional 2016, brokers said, but it has continued to scoop up space. LondonMetric said last week that it had leased warehouse space to Amazon.

LondonMetric aims to increase its logistics property holdings to 80% from about 60%, with a particular focus on properties in urban areas, Mr. Jones said. The company last week raised GBP 95.5 million ($119.9 million) through a share placing to fund such acquisitions.

Warehouses near cities are known as the "last mile" of a delivery network, which has become increasingly important to firms such as Amazon offering same-day services.

Blackstone Group LP, among the most active investors in European warehouses, has picked up on the trend. The U.S. private-equity firm last week agreed to buy, alongside U.K.-based real-estate group M7 Real Estate, a portfolio of last-mile warehouses in Germany and the Netherlands for 1.28 billion euros, according to Hansteen Holdings, the seller.

Blackstone also is selling its expansive European warehouse arm, Logicor. The private-equity firm is marketing the sale at 13 billion euros, while simultaneously working on an initial public offering. While the sale is Blackstone's preferred route, experts said, either path would result in one of the biggest real-estate transactions ever in Europe.

In one of the biggest European real-estate deals last year, GIC Pte. Ltd., Singapore's sovereign-wealth fund, bought warehouse-property company P3 Logistic Parks for 2.4 billion euros.

Strong investor demand has sent yields sharply lower. The average capitalization rate, a measure of yield, on European industrial properties was 6.4% in the fourth quarter last year, falling from 7.8% three years earlier, according to Real Capital Analytics.

"E-commerce has changed our markets globally," said Jack Cox, head of industrial and logistics capital markets at property broker CBRE. "And it's only just starting."

HFT as an insight into where fintech is going

Izabella Kaminska 
1,255 words
28 March 2017
10:18
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Markets are cyclical, Darwinian and subject to diminishing returns. Nothing new in that observation. But people do seem to forget it all the time.

Case in point: high-frequency trading (HFT). Circa 2009-2010, algorithmic trading techniques were considered a veritable goldmine for those who knew and understood how to deploy them against dumb human competitors.

But as with most innovations, markets evolved. Dumb competitors got wise and began to employ similar strategies to defend their wealth stores. A rush of new competitors meanwhile turned up to eat the lunches of all the first entrants. In no time at all, the lucrative profits to be had from HFT began to encounter limits (fast-as-light trading, regulation, et cetera) bringing forward a new equilibrium.

Now, tellingly, with their profits under threat, HFT companies are looking to invest in new techniques to recapture their edge. Much of this is focused on big data analytics, and of course AI and machine learning.

In a "http://streetwiseprofessor.com/?p=10451" Streetwise Professor Craig Pirrong, a market structure expert from the University of Houston, makes a valuable point about the nature of these sorts of cyclical transitions. Namely, they always happen. So don t be surprised when they do.

Remarking on Alex Osipovich s story about HFT traders "https://www.wsj.com/articles/high-frequency-traders-fall-on-hard-times-1490092200", "https://www.wsj.com/articles/virtu-financial-makes-bid-to-acquire-kcg-holdings-1489609032", Quantlabs "https://www.ft.com/content/a6ac732e-0e4b-11e7-a88c-50ba212dce4d" and Interactive Brokers "https://www.wsj.com/articles/options-trading-pioneer-blames-retreat-on-fast-trading-sharpshooters-1489099725", he notes, with our emphasis:

Alex s story repeats Tabb Group data documenting a roughly 85 percent drop in HFT revenues in US equity trading. The Virtu-KCG proposed tie-up and the Quantlabs-Teza consummated one are indications of consolidation that is typical of maturing industries, and a shift it the business model of these firms. The Quantlabs-Teza story is particularly interesting. It suggests that it is no longer possible (or at least remunerative) to get a competitive edge via speed alone. Instead, the focus is shifting to extracting information from the vast flow of data generated in modern markets. Speed will matter here he who analyzes faster, all else equal, will have an edge. But the margin for innovation will shift from hardware to data analytics software (presumably paired with specialized hardware optimized to use it).

What s interesting is what Pirrong goes on to say about "https://ftalphaville.ft.com/2010/06/15/260141/all-eyes-on-broker-dealer-internalisation/", incentives to keep investing in information collection and the related "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grossman-Stiglitz_Paradox".

The short story is this. As markets get increasingly competitive they encounter physical limits, such as fast-as-light trading. At this point it sometimes becomes cost effective to invest in protectionist inefficiencies. The illegal version of this comes in the shape of cornering, manipulation or front-running. The legal version, on the other hand, can take the form of further fragmentation, dark pools or internalisation   a euphemism for doing as much business as possible in-house and out of sight of the public market (ideally without ever taking any of it onto a public market at all).

A key requisite for establishing a  dark  internalised market is access to reliable retail flow that can be easily offset with more informed flow to someone s corresponding advantage or disadvantage. If internalised flow was both balanced and perfectly symmetrical in terms of information advantage, there d be little to no incentive to provide that market matching function in the first place. Money is made by knowing exclusively what portion of flow is failing to match at any given moment and taking the temporal position. This is information the internaliser has which no-one else does.

Informed entities understandably don t like this state of affairs since it allows less informed entities to free-ride on their information collection efforts. What s more, once their trades are run through the internaliser or HFT, the overall price signals are dampened in the public market to the disadvantage of the informed traders. Eventually, this reduces their incentive to keep digging for info. Accordingly, there is some controversy over how economically constructive the practice generally is.

Nevertheless, Pirrong stresses, there s a good argument to be made that all informed trading is really rent seeking anyway: resources spent on information collection for temporal forecasting advantage are always wasteful since the information is going to come out soon anyway. Meanwhile, adverse selection can set in, threatening the wider efficiency of the market. In the classic case, retail is put off from trading at all because they know they don t know what better funded and equipped traders might know.

Quid pro quo, if HFT market-makers or internalisers scoop up some of those rents for themselves they not only potentially put off investment in what was a wasteful activity to begin with but diminish the overall adverse selection effect in the market as a whole, encouraging retail punters to properly express themselves without fear of being taken advantage of.

As Pirrong notes:

 &by reducing the ability of informed traders to extract rents from uninformed traders, internalization (and dark markets) reduce the incentives to invest excessively in information collection (an incentive Hirshleifer the Elder noted in the 1970s).

What s all this got to do with fintech, you might ask? Well, if by fintech we understand an opportunity to invest in computer intensive analytical processes which cultivate information advantages for those doing the investing (from insurance companies, credit provision agencies to retail banks and hedge funds), then it indicates we have probably come full circle.

Put differently, market dynamics which previously incentivised a transfer of wealth from informed traders to less informed traders (to the retail user s cost advantage) are no longer cost effective. Since it no longer pays to benefit from someone else s information collection efforts, the disruptor must either turn to data collection and analysis directly (becoming the new informed entity) or quit the market altogether with everyone else, allowing inefficiencies to reemerge in the public market once again.

Either way, the fintech narrative implies that for rentier returns to continue at any significant level, huge amounts of investment are now needed in mass data collection and analysis.

But if Pirrong is right, all this really cultivates is an environment which encourages more adverse selection not less. In the worst case scenario, those who know they re being disadvantaged withdraw from the market completely, sabotage the data as best they can or   one might imagine   openly revolt against the system altogether (say by finding a way to nab some of the returns for themselves HFT-style or voting in powers which promise to protect them no matter what).

Which begs the question: what really is the most cost effective way to compete with entities who have bagged all the data, all the processing power and the smartest of algorithms or to disempower them?

If it s the cultivation of non-compliant, anonymous, privacy-obsessed dark markets which will be impossible to penetrate: "http://dealbreaker.com/2017/03/bitcoin-existential-crisis/"

If on the other hand it s the cultivation of a new breed of fintechs which can use brand popularity to get an edge on data collection, then that seems to be exactly what s happening now. Problem is   as with HFT   if and when everyone starts doing it, diminishing returns will lead to another scenario "https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NHWjlCaIrQo"

Related links:

"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kC72nnR8EW8"   FT

"https://ftalphaville.ft.com/2014/02/20/1777502/god-complex-alert/"   FT Alphaville

"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NHWjlCaIrQo"   Youtube  

LEX COLUMN
Tronc/Oaktree: Trib tribulations

345 words
28 March 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
10
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Private equity is generally the winner when embroiled in a boardroom brawl. Tronc, the media company once known more sensibly as Tribune Publishing, spent most of last year fighting off bids from Gannett, a rival publisher.

Now its two largest investors are at odds with each other. Shareholders should follow the example of the third biggest - Oaktree Capital - and sell.

A lengthy bidding process in 2016 culminated in a tentative deal at $18.75 a share, only for Gannett's bankers to then withdraw support. During that saga, Tronc chairman Michael Ferro enlisted the help of biotech magnate Patrick Soon-Shiong to fend off Gannett's overtures, citing his experience in artificial intelligence.

But relations have soured. Mr Soon-Shiong is reported to be shocked by Mr Ferro's behaviour and a perceived lack of independence at Tronc. In response, he has increased his own stake in Tronc to 24 per cent.

But Mr Ferro has upped the ante. Using shareholders' money, the company has spent $56m buying Oaktree's shares in Tronc at $15 each, a 12 per cent premium. If Tronc is subsequently acquired, Oaktree is owed the difference between that buyout price and the price it obtains for the remainder of its stake. This exercise has cost Tronc nearly a third of its gross cash balance (it also has $370m of debt). The company has lifted the ownership ceiling for Mr Ferro but not for his rival. Mr Soon-Shiong has been sidelined. Tronc has removed him from its list of director nominees. Perhaps the company will offer to buy his stake now, too.

Ordinary shareholders know Tronc's managers made a mess of the bidding process and are now spending money entrenching themselves. Investors also know they are unlikely to get as good a deal as Oaktree did on its way out.

Even so, Tronc's shares are up 40 per cent since the deal died. Given the actions of the board, selling is now a sensible choice.

COMPANIES
BoE set to raise the bar on resilience; Banks: Stress tests

By Emma Dunkley in London 
621 words
28 March 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
12
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Institutions to be checked against long-term challenges and consumer credit risks

The UK's largest banks face their toughest test of resilience yet after the central bank unveiled an extra assessment of their business models and separately launched a review into lenders' consumer credit standards.

The Bank of England will put the seven largest lenders - including Lloyds Banking Group, HSBC, Barclays and Royal Bank of Scotland - under further examination alongside its annual stress test, which measures how banks would fare in certain conditions, at the end of this year.

The new "exploratory" test, which will be carried out every other year, will assess banks' resilience to a wider range of risks beyond those emanating from the financial cycle - such as persistently low interest rates and high costs.

The longer-term test involves banks submitting projections for seven years and will help the BoE identify how the financial system will be reshaped if, for example, there are persistent headwinds to bank profitability. The BoE will not publish details of the results on a bank-by-bank basis.

The new assessment will include weak global growth, continuing low interest rates, falling world trade, increased competitive pressure on large UK banks and misconduct costs.

One adviser said no other central bank had launched such a test before and that it was "uncharted waters for both the BoE and banks".

David Strachan, a head of regulatory strategy at Deloitte, said of this year's assessment: "I think the way that it tests business models and capital levels . . . in terms of organisation and administration and logistics then it is more challenging this time around."

Lee Thorpe, a manager at SAS UK and Ireland, an analytics company, added there would be more pressure on banks as a result of the new test. "A substantial change in approach to identify risks in the business model may require an alternative methodology, further challenging banks to deliver in tight timeframes," he said.

The BoE also said yesterday that its Prudential Regulation Authority would launch a review into banks' lending standards in relation to consumer credit following soaring growth in the sector.

The review will focus on underwriting standards and the risk models employed by banks.

Consumer credit is one of the largest risks on a bank's balance sheet. Last year, UK banks had 19bn of impairments on credit cards, compared with 12bn on mortgages.

The BoE's Financial Policy Committee said it would also oversee banks' contingency plans to mitigate risks as the process for withdrawing from the EU unfolded. It said: "Risks to financial stability will be influenced by the orderliness of the adjustment to the new relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union."

The BoE is including for the first time an "anchor" on levels of severity used within its annual stress test. This means it will only get more severe in areas where the BoE thinks risks have increased. The test, the results of which will be published in the fourth quarter, will examine how lenders deal with a rise in UK unemployment to 9.5 per cent - the same as last year - and a 33 per cent drop in house prices, slightly more than the previous year's scenario.

The bank is testing against a hypothetical 40 per cent decline in commercial real estate , compared with 42 per cent the previous year.

But the global economic scenario will be made more severe, with global gross domestic product contracting by 2.4 per cent and Chinese GDP by 1.2 per cent, implying that the BoE sees global economic hurdles as a greater threat.

High-profile venture-capital firms have put a total of $69.2 million behind an effort to be the first company to develop a viable business around quantum computing.

Rigetti Computing announced that it raised $64 million in Series A and B funding. Andreessen Horowitz led a $24 million Series A, and Vy Capital led a $40 million Series B, bringing the company's total funding to $69.2 million. It isn't clear when the rounds closed.

Major investors in both rounds included AME Cloud Ventures, Data Collective, FF Science, Morado Ventures, Western Technology Investment and Y Combinator through its Continuity Fund. Institutional investors in the Series A included Bloomberg Beta, Lux Capital, Streamlined Ventures, Susa Ventures and Sutter Hill Ventures.

Rigetti Computing, formally known as Rigetti & Co., was founded by Chief Executive Chad Rigetti in 2013 and has offices in Fremont and Berkeley, Calif. The company said it is building a cloud quantum computing platform for artificial intelligence and computational chemistry.

The concept of quantum computing, in which the properties of quantum mechanics are harnessed for computing power, has been around for decades. Only more recently have companies begun attempting to use quantum computing to solve complex problems.

The Roundup

U.S. & Canada

Buyouts

Alpine Investors, San Francisco

Alpine Investors has collected more than $300 million so far for Alpine Investors VI LP, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund, which has a $500 million target and a $525 million hard cap, is expected to hold a final closing in the second quarter, the person said. If the firm gathers the amount it is seeking, the pool will be a step up in size from its predecessor, a $406 million fund raised in 2014. Alpine invests in founder-owned U.S. companies in the software and business-services sectors as well as in online enterprises such as publishers, media businesses and digital marketplaces, according to its website. The firm aims to back companies with $5 million to $15 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, though it can invest in software businesses with as little as $1 million in Ebitda.

Apollo Global Management LLC, New York

Apollo Global Management LLC has collected at least $360.4 million so far for its maiden special situations fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The fund, Apollo Special Situations Fund LP, is seeking $750 million for opportunities that fall outside the mandate of the firm's flagship buyout fund. Joshua Harris, co-founder and senior managing director of Apollo, said on a call with analysts last year that the fund will target investments in "longer dated private-equity, royalties, infrastructure and minority investments." The vehicle held a first closing on $250 million in May. Apollo already has begun investing the fund. In August, Apollo entered into a partnership with sportswear company Nike Inc., through which the firm and footwear maker said they would increase manufacturing capabilities in the Americas.

Blackstone Group LP, New York

Blackstone Group LP has collected $5 billion for its first longer-life fund, a person familiar with the matter told Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News. The fund has hit its target and isn't likely to raise more capital, though it hasn't held a final close, the person said. The vehicle has a 20-year lifespan compared with a typical fund life of 10 years. It also will invest in companies for more than twice the typical three-to-five-year hold period, the person said. The fund will target less than the usual 20% internal rate of return a private-equity fund aims for, Private Equity Analyst previously reported.

Edgewater Funds, Chicago

Edgewater Funds is seeking $1 billion for its latest fund, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The firm in the filings didn't record any capital commitments to Edgewater Growth Capital Partners IV LP and a parallel vehicle. The fund's predecessor was seeking $650 million and had gathered at least $363.2 million by June 2011, according to a separate filing. It isn't clear on what amount that fund ultimately closed. Edgewater makes both buyout and growth-equity investments in midmarket companies. The firm manages $2.4 billion, according to its website. Among its recent activity, Edgewater in December said it sold Southern Petroleum Laboratories, a Houston company that performs outsourced hydrocarbon measurement, analysis and reporting services for oil and gas producers, to Industrial Growth Partners.

Goldman Sachs Group Inc., New York

Petershill, a group within Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s asset management arm, is pitching its first fund devoted to taking minority positions in private-equity shops, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund, which is branded as Petershill Private Equity, is seeking $2 billion and closed on $950 million in February. The vehicle will hunt for stakes in smaller private-equity firms poised to become marquee names in the asset management world. It could offer to buy founders' stakes, spreading the wealth of these firms more evenly to help a new generation of deal makers rise. Goldman's Petershill unit is considering taking a pool of its stakes in private-equity firms public in five to seven years. It also could sell the portfolio to a large strategic buyer. The pool will take passive stakes of 10% to 30% in the management companies of firms, mostly focusing on those with assets of $5 billion to $15 billion, the person said. The fund will have no deadline by which it must sell its holdings.

HealthpointCapital is raising its fourth fund for transactions in the musculoskeletal sector, the only market in which it invests. The firm said the new vehicle will target both emerging businesses and established companies. HealthpointCapital said it also would "seek out established but undernourished segments and products" that are owned by medical technology companies. HealthpointCapital currently has $800 million under investment.

J.F. Lehman & Co. raised $833 million for its latest fund, exceeding a $700 million target. The vehicle will target controlling positions in companies based in the U.S. and U.K. The fund's predecessor closed with commitments of $575.5 million in 2011.

KKR & Co. has closed its newest flagship fund with $13.9 billion. The firm itself invested about $1.4 billion in the new fund. The vehicle, KKR Americas Fund XII LP, came in ahead of the firm's previous flagship fund, which closed with around $8.7 billion, according to public filings. The fund's predecessor closed on $1.57 billion in 2013 to invest alongside private-equity investors. Lexington's co-investment strategy has attracted backing from investors including the Florida State Board of Administration, according to the firm's website. Through the strategy, Lexington targets $10 million to $100 million per transaction. The firm also makes primary and secondary fund investments.

Marlin Equity Partners has held final closings for two funds totaling $3.25 billion. Marlin Equity V LP, the firm's fifth flagship fund, gathered $2.5 billion for midmarket investments; and Marlin Heritage II LP collected $750 million for lower midmarket deals. Marlin Equity's fourth flagship fund closed on $1.6 billion in 2013. Its debut Heritage fund closed on $400 million in 2014. The two previous funds were raised in tandem, with the lower midmarket vehicle offered only to investors in the flagship fund. The Ohio Police & Fire Pension Fund is an investor in the new funds, according to a pension document.
