The Heritage strategy takes the same approach but is limited to North America and targets smaller investments of $10 million to $60 million, according to the document.

Platinum Equity, Beverly Hills, Calif.

Platinum Equity raised $6.5 billion for Platinum Equity Capital Partners IV LP, a private-equity fund that will invest globally. The vehicle, the largest fund the firm has raised, will target corporate carve-outs; public-to-private deals; and secondary acquisitions. Its predecessor was a $3.75 billion pool for which the firm announced a final closing in 2013. The latest fund attracted commitments from more than 200 investors from 28 countries.

Quad-C Management, Charlottesville, Va.

Quad-C Management hit its hard cap of $1.1 billion in limited partner commitments for Quad-C Partners IX LP, exceeding an $800 million target for the fund. The vehicle received a $56 million general partner commitment in addition to the amount raised from investors, a firm spokeswoman said. The firm expects the new fund to close its first deal soon, the spokeswoman said. The fund's predecessor, which was raised in 2012, gathered $700 million. Quad-C buys ownership stakes in North American midmarket companies in the business-services, consumer, industrial, health-care, distribution, transportation and logistics industries. It invests $35 million to $125 million of equity at a time in companies valued at $75 million to $400 million, according to its website. The firm in February announced an investment in Pharm-Olam International Ltd., which offers services for clinical trials.

Quantum Energy Partners, Houston

Quantum Energy Partners is looking for more capital to invest in the oil patch with plans to raise about $4.3 billion for new deals. The oil-and-gas investment firm is targeting $3.5 billion for Quantum Energy Partners VII LP, said five people familiar with the matter. The fund is expected to hold a first closing in April, said three of the people. Quantum is seeking another $750 million in co-investment dollars to bolster its spending power, said two of the people. Quantum in 2015 held a final closing for its sixth private-equity fund at $4.45 billion. That pool is nearly fully committed to deals, said two of the people. Quantum typically invests $200 million to $500 million per transaction and can deploy up to $1 billion in certain deals, said three of the people. The firm does deals in the upstream, midstream, oil-field services and power sectors.

River Associates Investments, Chattanooga, Tenn.

River Associates Investments aims to raise $275 million for River VII LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm closed its sixth fund in 2012 with $222 million, exceeding an initial target of $200 million. River Associates invests in U.S. and Canadian manufacturing, distribution, industrial services and retail companies with $3 million to $12 million in annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. The firm in February 2016 said it acquired Weaver Leather LLC, a maker of stirrups and saddle pads for horses as well as pet and livestock accessories.

Wellspring Capital Management, New York

Wellspring Capital Management is seeking a cumulative $1.2 billion for Wellspring Capital Partners VI LP and two related vehicles, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The midmarket investor's previous fund closed on $1.2 billion in 2011. Wellspring Capital Partners V LP had a 15.1% net internal rate of return as of June 30, according to a pension document from the California Public Employees' Retirement System, a fund backer. Earlier this year, Wellspring said it agreed to sell outsourced skilled labor provider Tradesmen International LLC to Blackstone Group LP. In November, it sold flexible packaging company ProAmpac to Pritzker Group Private Capital.

Funds of Funds

Fund Evaluation Group LLC, Cincinnati

An affiliate of investment consulting firm Fund Evaluation Group LLC said it held a final closing on $194.5 million for its third private-capital fund of funds. The affiliate, FEG Private Investors, collected more than the $175 million it was targeting for the vehicle, FEG Private Opportunities Fund III LP. The fund also gathered more capital than FEG Private Investors' second pool, which wrapped up in 2015 with $227.3 million. The investor's first fund closed with $122.3 million in 2012. The latest vehicle, as with its predecessor, can invest in private-equity, private-debt and real-assets strategies.

Venture Investment Associates, Peapack, N.J.

Venture Investment Associates, a private-equity fund of funds, has raised more than $230 million across multiple vehicles. The firm gathered total commitments of more than $150 million for Venture Investment Associates VIII LP, which targets venture-capital, growth-equity and buyout funds, and VIA VIII-VC LP, which focuses exclusively on seed- and early-stage managers. The firm also closed on $80 million for its third Seed Technology Partners funds, which include both VIA Seed Technology Partners Platform Fund LP for established funds and VIA Seed Technology Partners Explorer Fund LP for emerging seed funds, which also can do direct co-investments with the funds it backs. The main funds are 20% larger than their prior funds, while the seed funds are 50% larger than their previous funds, said Jason Andris, a managing director at the firm.

Western & Southern Financial Group, Cincinnati

Fort Washington Capital Partners Group, the private-equity division of Western & Southern Financial Group, reached the goal for its ninth fund of funds, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Fort Washington Private Equity Investors IX LP and two parallel vehicles have collected $300 million in total, according to the filings. That figure matches the total offering amount listed on earlier SEC filings. The filings don't state whether the fund has reached a final close. The previous fund in the series closed on $300 million in 2015. Public pensions that committed to the ninth fund include the Kentucky Teachers' Retirement System, the Public School Retirement System of the City of St. Louis and the City of Cincinnati Retirement System, according to pension documents. Fort Washington's funds of funds aim for fund managers in buyout, growth, distressed and credit funds, and seeks to allocate 15% of the fund to secondary deals, according to a pension document.

Mezzanine & Other Debt

Cerberus Capital Management, New York

Cerberus Capital Management affiliate Cerberus Business Finance held a final closing for its latest midmarket direct-lending vehicle, Cerberus Levered Loan Opportunities Fund III LP, at $2.05 billion. The vehicle surpassed a target range of $1.5 billion to $2 billion. Cerberus Business Finance historically has focused on originating senior secured loans primarily to U.S. midmarket companies. Cerberus Capital Management targets transactions through private-equity and real-estate strategies.

Venture Capital

Alta Partners, San Francisco

Alta Partners is aiming to raise $200 million for its ninth fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The health-care focused firm didn't record any commitments to Alta Partners IX LP in the filing. Alta Partners wrapped up its eighth venture-capital fund at $500 million in 2006, Private Equity Analyst previously reported.

BioInnovation Capital, Cambridge, Mass.

BioInnovation Capital has raised at least $65.6 million for BioInnovation Capital I LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The early-stage life-sciences investor has backed Sentien Biotechnologies Inc., a business focused on novel approaches to cell therapy, and Avexegen Therapeutics Inc., a company working on a drug candidate, Neuregulin-4, aimed at patients with Crohn's disease and ulcerative colitis.

Biomatics Capital Partners, Seattle

Biomatics Capital Partners, led by co-founders with ties to Bill Gates, has raised a $200 million debut fund to back startups in genomics, digital health and data-driven health-care technologies. Formed last year, the firm closed the fund above a $150 million target. Biomatics co-founder Boris Nikolic formerly was chief adviser for science and technology to Mr. Gates. Co-founder Julie Sunderland was a director of program-related investments for the Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation. Before hitting the fundraising trail, both co-founders invested their own money in eight companies, all of which have since been rolled into the Biomatics pool, Ms. Sunderland said.

Blumberg Capital, San Francisco

Blumberg Capital has raised more than $200 million for its fourth fund. The firm plans to invest Blumberg Capital IV LP in areas including digital security, financial technology and big data. The new capital will enable the firm to strike early-stage deals and do follow-on investments, said founder and Managing Partner David Blumberg. The firm plans to continue writing checks of $500,000 to $5 million, he said, and will consider special purpose vehicle funds as it has done in the past. Blumberg Capital closed its third fund at $150 million in 2013. The firm was an early investor in recently public Nutanix Inc., a data center infrastructure company, and Hootsuite Media Inc., which offers a social media management platform.

Corazon Capital, Chicago

Corazon Capital has closed its second fund at $40 million. The firm was launched by OkCupid co-founder and former Match Group Inc. Chief Executive Sam Yagan, who recently took the helm at ShopRunner Inc., a membership company that provides two-day shipping. The firm said it deployed its first fund, an $11.4 million vehicle, in equal thirds in the Midwest, East Coast and West Coast. More than half of the first-time investments Corazon made from its debut fund were in seed-stage rounds, but Mr. Yagan said the firm will dip its toe more in Series A funding in Chicago.

Double M Partners, Los Angeles

Double M Partners has received commitments of at least $18.3 million so far for Double M Partners III LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The fund is aiming to raise $40 million, the filing indicates. Double M invests in internet, media and communications companies working on business-to-business offerings in areas including infrastructure, software, security, management platforms and hardware, according to its website.

Hummer Winblad Venture Partners, San Francisco

Hummer Winblad Venture Partners is seeking to raise $125 million for its latest fund, Hummer Winblad Venture Partners VII LP. The early-stage enterprise-software investor has closed on about $30 million so far for the vehicle, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm raised its last fund in 2007 and closed on at least $198 million, SEC filings indicate. Hummer Winblad's active portfolio companies include software makers MuleSoft Inc., Aria Systems Inc. and OptiMine Software Inc.

K2 Global, San Francisco

K2 Global, a venture firm with partners in Silicon Valley and Singapore, closed its first fund with $183 million. Founded in 2015, the firm so far has deployed $58 million in more than 10 companies. Deals include investments in U.S.-based companies such as Magic Leap Inc., a maker of augmented reality technology, and Uber Technologies Inc., as well as Paktor Pte. Ltd. in Asia. Former startup lawyer Minal Hasan founded the firm with Ozi Amanant, who previously worked at a family office in Singapore. More than half of investments will occur at later stages, Ms. Hasan said.

Lemnos, San Francisco

Lemnos said it raised $50 million for its third fund focused on hardware investments. The firm typically invests $500,000 to $3 million in preseed and seed rounds that it leads or co-leads. It closed its previous fund with $20 million in 2014 and has made investments in 39 companies since its founding in 2011.

Lux Capital, New York

Lux Capital has raised $400 million for its fifth fund, Lux Ventures V LP. The firm aims to invest in seed- and early-stage companies focused on science and technology developments in areas such as 3-D printing, artificial intelligence, drones, computational imaging, synthetic biology, virtual reality and genomics, among others. It closed its previous fund at $350 million in 2015.

Next Coast Ventures, Austin, Texas

Next Coast Ventures closed its debut fund at $85 million. The firm aims to back software-focused startups and companies in the business-to-business, business-to-consumer, retail and education sectors. It so far has invested in four Austin, Texas, startups, including Dropoff Inc., OnRamp Access LLC, Umuse and Phlur Inc., as well as Cloverpop Inc. in San Francisco and Clarity Money Inc. in New York. Next Coast has made another investment in San Francisco that it hasn't yet announced, said Mike Smerklo, a former chief executive of ServiceSource International Inc. who founded the firm in 2015 with Tom Ball. Mr. Ball previously was a general partner at Austin Ventures. Next Coast plans to invest $2 million to $5 million per transaction at Series A and Series B stages, Mr. Smerklo said.

NextGen Venture Partners, Washington

NextGen Venture Partners has closed its first fund at $22 million. The firm said it has lined up a network of nearly 700 venture partners to assist it with identifying and supporting portfolio companies. It said it plans to increase that number to 1,000. The fund so far has invested in 17 early-stage companies. The firm aims to lead seed rounds of up to $1.5 million and participates in Series A investments. It targets investments of $500,000 in U.S.-based companies.

Notation Capital, Brooklyn, N.Y.

Notation Capital is raising a second fund, said two people familiar with the matter. The firm is seeking to raise a $30 million vehicle, one person said. A Securities and Exchange Commission filing indicates the firm is raising Notation Capital II LP with a $25 million offering amount. Notation is a New York-focused investor that backs emerging startups in preseed rounds. It may expand outside of the city to look for deals in the future, one person said. The firm launched in early 2015 and gathered about $8 million for its first fund.

Ribbit Capital, Palo Alto, Calif.

Financial-technology investor Ribbit Capital is looking to raise $300 million for Ribbit Capital IV LP. The firm didn't disclose any commitments to the fund in a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Its previous fund was seeking $220 million in 2015, according to a separate filing. It isn't clear on what amount that fund held a final closing. Ribbit's investments include financial-planning business Wealthfront Inc., stock-trading startup Robinhood Markets Inc. and Credit Karma Inc., which lets consumers see their credit scores for free.

TrueBridge Capital Partners, Chapel Hill, N.C.

TrueBridge Capital Partners closed its first direct-investment fund with $125 million in commitments, surpassing a $100 million target. TrueBridge Direct Fund LP will focus on mid- to late-stage U.S. technology companies. Its previous four funds were venture-capital funds of funds, the latest of which closed with $400 million in 2016.

Wildcat Venture Partners, San Mateo, Calif.

Wildcat Venture Partners raised $57 million for its inaugural fund, Wildcat Technology Partners 2015 LP. The firm was seeking $100 million for the fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing dated Dec. 16. Wildcat backs companies in the consumer, digital-health, education-technology, online marketplace, financial-technology, marketing-technology and enterprise sectors. The firm has been investing for about a year and a half and so far has backed about 20 companies, said Bruce Cleveland, a Wildcat founding partner.

Europe

Buyouts

Apax Partners MidMarket, Paris

Apax Partners MidMarket raised  1 billion ($1.06 billion) for its ninth fund, Apax France IX. The firm collected 20% of the vehicle's capital from North America, 31% from Europe and 49% from France. The fund will invest in French and other European small and midsize enterprises of up to  500 million in value in four sectors: technology, media and telecommunications; retail and consumer goods; health care; and services. The firm said the pool is 23% invested across three companies: InfoVista SAS, a provider of network performance software services; Sandaya SAS, which manages campsites in France and Spain; and Marlink SAS, which provides mobile-satellite communication services. Apax France IX investors co-invested  190 million in the three deals. The fund's predecessor wrapped up with  704 million in 2011.

Apis Partners, London

Apis Partners held a $287 million final closing for its first private-equity fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund closed above its $250 million target, after holding a first closing on more than $157 million in August 2015. It received backing from financial institutions including, HarbourVest Partners, African Development Bank, Prudential Financial Inc. and Italian banking group Intesa Sanpaolo SpA. The vehicle, which will target financial-services deals in Africa and South Asia, so far has invested $130 million across five transactions. It recently acquired a minority stake in cross-border money transfer provider TransFast for an undisclosed sum, a person familiar with the matter said.

Ardian, Paris

Ardian is talking with investors about raising a fifth co-investment fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm is looking to gather about  1.1 billion ($1.16 billion) in total for the strategy across two separate pools. One of the pools will give investors access to co-investment opportunities in the U.S., and the other will provide access to the European market, the person said. The fund is expected to be roughly the same size as its predecessor, which closed on  1.1 billion in 2015. Ardian's co-investment team commits  20 million to  50 million per transaction. It also can participate in European deals as a minority investor. The firm launched its co-investment strategy in 2005 and has since invested in 50 companies globally, according to its website.

EMK Capital, London

EMK Capital aims to raise $683.3 million for its maiden buyout fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. EMK Capital Partners LP will take majority stakes in companies across the business services, consumer and industrial sectors and can invest up to 100 million ($121.7 million) of equity per deal, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. EMK was set up last year by Mark Joseph, a former Oakley Capital partner, and Bregal Capital co-founder Edmund Lazarus.

EQT, Stockholm

EQT gathered  4 billion ($4.21 billion) for its third infrastructure fund. EQT Infrastructure III closed above its  2.9 billion target, said a person familiar with the matter. Around two-thirds of the commitments were made by investors in previous EQT funds. Investors in the vehicle include French private-equity investor Ardian, pension fund New York City Retirement Systems and asset manager BlackRock Inc. The fund, which so far has made four acquisitions, will seek investments in North America and Europe.

HgCapital, London

HgCapital held a final closing for its latest flagship buyout fund, HgCapital 8, at 2.5 billion ($3.11 billion). Through its buyout funds, the firm targets deals throughout the U.K. Germany and the Nordic region in sectors including industrials; services; and technology, media, and telecommunications. HgCapital also held a first and final closing for its second lower midmarket, TMT-focused fund, HgCapital Mercury 2, at 575 million. Investment trust HgCapital Trust PLC provided the largest commitment to both funds. Their predecessors closed on 2 billion and 380 million, respectively, in 2013.

Nexxus Capital, Mexico City

Mexican firm Nexxus Capital is raising a new fund to invest in companies in Spain that later could expand back in Mexico. Nexxus has set a  200 million ($212.6 million) target for the vehicle and is raising it through Nexxus Iberia SGEIC SA, a Madrid-based entity the firm formed last year in partnership with two local private-equity executives, said Roberto Terrazas de la Cerda, a Nexxus managing director and chief investor relations officer. Nexxus Iberia plans to hold a first closing for Nexxus Iberia Fund I FCR by the middle of the year and a final closing by the end of 2017 or early 2018, Mr. de la Cerda said. Backers of the fund include Fond-ICO Global, a Spanish government-backed fund of funds, he said. Nexxus's Spanish arm will target local midsize companies with annual revenue of  50 million to  200 million that show potential to later expand into Mexico, possibly through mergers with Mexican businesses the firm already owns, Mr. de la Cerda said. The firm closed its latest buyout fund, Nexxus Capital Private Equity Fund VI LP, in 2013 at $550 million, according to its website, and has just launched its first mezzanine vehicle.

Summa Equity, Stockholm

Summa Equity held a final closing for its first buyout vehicle at 4.5 billion Swedish kronor ($505.7 million), exceeding its original target of SEK3.3 billion. The fund will invest in companies in the Nordic lower midmarket sector. The vehicle was backed by investors including endowments, foundations, pension funds, insurance firms and funds of funds from throughout the Nordic region, Europe and North America. Summa has executed two deals so far using capital from the fund, including an investment in Sortera Skandinavien Holding AB, a Swedish waste and recycling collector.

True Capital, London

True Capital, a firm focused exclusively on the retail and consumer sectors, held a final closing for its second buyout fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund raised a little more than 100 million ($122 million), more than double the size of its maiden vehicle, which closed at 40 million in 2015, the person said. True Capital received backing for the latest fund from institutional investors including the London Pensions Fund Authority, the pension fund of supermarket giant Tesco PLC and private-equity investor Adveq Management, according to filings with Companies House. Alongside the firm's growth and buyout operations, True Capital also runs TrueStart, an accelerator that provides funding, strategic advice and a network for early-stage companies in the firm's target sectors.

Funds of Funds

Adveq Management, Zurich

Adveq Management is looking to collect $250 million for its latest technology-focused investment pool, Adveq Technology IX SCS, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm's previous technology vehicles targeted fund-of-funds investments in U.S. venture- and growth-capital pools as well as secondary deals and co-investments. Adveq also is seeking $265.4 million for a vehicle called Adveq Europe Direct II SCS, according to a separate SEC filing. The firm didn't record any commitments to either vehicle. Adveq has held a number of fund closings recently, including a final closing on  462 million for Adveq Europe VI in late 2016. That fund targets primary, secondary and co-investment opportunities in the European small-cap sector. The firm also closed two funds in early 2016, raising a combined  425 million. One was a  323 million fund to invest in private-equity managers. The other was a  102 million fund dedicated to co-investments in Europe.

Secondary

Idinvest Partners, Paris

Idinvest Partners is expected to close its third secondary fund imminently, above the upper end of its target range of  350 million ($375.8 million) to  400 million, Chief Executive and Managing Partner Christophe Bavire told sister publication Private Equity News. The oversubscribed fund is expected to collect  420 million to  440 million total, Mr. Bavire said. A spokeswoman for the fund said the vehicle doesn't have a hard cap. Idinvest's third secondary fund will be about twice the size of the firm's last secondary vehicle, which wrapped up with  214 million in 2014. The latest fund so far is about 30% invested, Mr. Bavire said. Its sweet spot for investments is  25 million to  40 million.

Venture Capital

Atomico, London

Atomico gathered $765 million for its fourth fund, Atomico IV LP. The Europe-focused early-stage investor so far has deployed the fund in businesses including Lilium GmbH, a plane maker that does business as Lilium Aviation; Pipedrive, a maker of customer relationship management software; and Bitmovin, which converts video and audio into live-streaming formats. Atomico closed the vehicle's predecessor at $476 million in 2013.

Bridge Ventures, London

Social impact investor Bridges Ventures plans to raise about 150 million ($186.7 million) for its fourth sustainable growth fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund, like its predecessors, will provide growth capital for small to midsize businesses predominantly based in the U.K. in the health and well-being, education and environmental sectors. Bridges' sustainable growth funds typically invest 2 million to 20 million in businesses. Bridges Sustainable Growth Fund III, its most recently closed vehicle in the series, completed fundraising with 125 million in 2013.

Project A Services, Berlin

Project A Services has closed on  140 million ($147.9 million) for its second fund. The early-stage venture investor in February also said it was about to close a vehicle with an additional  40 million dedicated to follow-on investments. Project A is backed by investors including retail business Otto Group, digital publishing company Axel Springer SE and the European Investment Fund.

Sofinnova Partners, Paris

Life-sciences investor Sofinnova Partners held a first closing on  106 million ($111.9 million) for a fund dedicated to industrial biotechnology. The vehicle, Sofinnova Industrial Biotech I, will back companies in sectors including renewable chemistry and synthetic biology. Through its renewable chemistry investments, the firm will invest in startups helping to turn renewable raw materials such as agricultural waste and carbon dioxide into renewable products like bioplastics. The fund will invest in a total of eight to 10 companies over the next three to four years. Limited partners include European institutions and players from the energy, chemical and agricultural sectors. Sofinnova, which currently has more than  1.6 billion under management, in 2015 held a final closing for Sofinnova Capital VIII at  300 million to invest within the health-care industry's biopharmaceutical and medical-device sectors.

Asia

Buyouts

Beijing Hosen Investment Management, Beijing

Beijing Hosen Investment Management, a Chinese firm that does business as Hosen Capital, wrapped up its third fund, Hosen Fund III, with investor commitments of $440 million. The vehicle received backing from investors in the U.S., Europe, Middle East and Asia. Hosen targets food and agribusiness companies located in China or operating in the market. The firm has $2.31 billion in assets under management and is backed by New Hope Group, an agribusiness conglomerate in China.

Venture Capital

Lilly Asia Ventures, Shanghai

Lilly Asia Ventures, a life-sciences investor backed by Eli Lilly & Co., aims to raise $450 million for a new fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm in the filing didn't record any capital commitments for LAV Biosciences Fund IV LP. Lilly Asia makes venture investments in the life-sciences and health-care sectors in Asia, with an emphasis on China. It typically deploys $5 million to $15 million per round for minority stakes. The firm in 2015 was seeking $150 million for LAV Biosciences Fund III LP, according to SEC filings. It isn't clear how much the fund ultimately raised.

Latin America

Buyouts

Actis, London

Actis closed its latest energy fund at its $2.75 billion hard cap. The firm, which looks for deals in regions including Africa, Asia and Latin America, exceeded a $2 billion target for Actis Energy 4 LP. The pool will seek controlling investments in the electricity generation and distribution sectors. It so far $2 billion of deal equity "either completed or in late stage," according to a news release. The amount includes $525 million deployed by the firm to establish a Pan-Latin American renewable energy platform, Atlas Renewable Energy, said Adiba Ighodaro, a partner who leads the firm's investor development group in the Americas. The firm announced the deal in February. Actis closed its previous energy fund at $1.15 billion in 2013. The firm has raised more than $12 billion since it was founded in 2004.

President Donald Trump nominated Scott Gottlieb, a venture capitalist and medical doctor with previous government experience, to run the Food and Drug Administration, the White House said. Dr. Gottlieb, who has ties to the drug industry, previously served as deputy FDA commissioner under President George W. Bush. He joined New Enterprise Associates as a venture partner in 2007.

Z Capital Group appointed Peter Coroneos as a managing director in the firm's New York office, where he will lead its corporate-development and investor-relations efforts. Most recently, Mr. Coroneos was a managing director of the private-capital group at Jefferies LLC. Before that, he held capital-raising roles at Avenue Capital Group, Himelsein Mandel Fund Management and Cullen Investments.

Trevor Oelschig, previously a partner at Bessemer Venture Partners, is joining General Catalyst as a managing director. Mr. Oelschig will focus on startups in enterprise and business applications at the firm, which is officially opening a San Francisco office. General Catalyst, which was founded in 2000 in Cambridge, Mass., opened a Silicon Valley office, in Palo Alto, Calif., in 2011. The firm now has five managing directors on the West Coast and is investing out of an $800 million 2016-vintage fund.

Globe-trotting 500 Startups has brought on Aman Verjee as its first chief operating officer, a move to help the frenetic seed investing firm manage a growing number of investments, employees and locations. Mr. Verjee joins 500 Startups after a tenure at CAN Capital, a venture-backed alternative lending platform.

HealthpointCapital, a private-equity firm that invests in the musculoskeletal sector, hired Mike Mogul as a managing director. Mr. Mogul was most recently president and chief executive of Blackstone Group LP-owned medical-device maker DJO Global Inc. Before joining DJO, he was group president of orthopedics for medical technology company Stryker Corp.

GTCR hired Jim Bonetti as a managing director to head its capital markets group. Mr. Bonetti previously was a managing director who co-headed the North American leveraged and acquisition finance group at Morgan Stanley. Before joining Morgan Stanley, he was an analyst in the equity capital markets and asset management groups at Goldman Sachs Group Inc.

Energy investor Talara Capital Management hired Jeff Gutke as a managing director. Mr. Gutke comes to Talara from fellow energy-focused private-equity firm Denham Capital Management, where he was a director. He has 22 years of experience in the energy industry, including stints at J.M. Huber Corp. and Exxon Mobil Corp.

Blockchain Capital, which invests exclusively in bitcoin and blockchain startups, hired a Needham & Co. analyst as a managing director. Spencer Bogart, who covered bitcoin and blockchain technology at the investment bank, also will be head of research for the venture firm. Blockchain Capital said it has invested in 42 startups in the blockchain technology sector.

Webb, a founding investing partner of Underscore.VC, is leaving the firm, she wrote in a blog post...Ringleader Ventures brought on Tony Burke, a veteran of the American Hospital Association, to co-lead its third fund, which is focused on startups serving health-care providers...Industry Ventures hired Jonathan Roosevelt as a venture partner. He most recently managed Roosevelt Capital.

BANK REGULATION

ECB Official Urges

More Mergers

Europe's top banking supervisor touted regulators' achievements in pulling eurozone banks back from the brink, though they said more should be done to improve the sector's profitability, repeating a call for more mergers among banks.

The eurozone economy is moving to slow, steady growth -- giving the region's banks a shot in the arm -- after a long contraction following the 2011 euro crisis.

"I hope [economic] recovery will help us to have even more success in the future," said Daniele Nouy, head of the supervisory board of the European Central Bank, speaking at a media conference.

Ms. Nouy runs the Single Supervisory Mechanism, the ECB's banking-regulation arm, which supervises nearly 130 of the largest lenders across the 19-country eurozone.

The ECB supervisor stressed the need for more mergers in the banking sector, noting that this would improve lenders' profitability. "We need consolidation of the banking system," she said, adding that this could take place either within a country or across borders. "It would make sense in certain cases to have cross-border consolidation."

-- Todd Buell

---

ENERGY

Oversupply Fears

Hold Gas in Check

Natural-gas prices fell, reversing early gains as fears of oversupply keep a rally in check.

Natural gas for April delivery settled down 2.4 cents, or 0.8%, at $3.052 a million British thermal units on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Prices rose as high as $3.124/mmBtu before flipping to losses late in the day.

UK banks face further scrutiny on top of annual stress test

Emma Dunkley 
729 words
27 March 2017
14:15

FTCMA
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The UK s largest banks face their toughest ever test of resilience after the central bank unveiled an extra assessment of their business models and separately launched a review into lenders  consumer credit standards.

The Bank of England will put the seven largest lenders   including Lloyds Banking Group, HSBC, Barclays and Royal Bank of Scotland   under further examination alongside its annual stress test, which measures how banks would fare in certain conditions, at the end of this year.

The new  exploratory  test, which will be carried out every other year, will assess banks  resilience to a wider range of risks beyond those emanating from the financial cycle   such as persistently low interest rates and high costs.

The longer-term test involves banks submitting projections for seven years and will help the BoE identify how the financial system will be reshaped if, for example, there are persistent headwinds to bank profitability. The BoE will not publish details of the results on a bank-by-bank basis.

The new assessment will include weak global growth, continuing low interest rates, falling world trade, increased competitive pressure on large UK banks and misconduct costs.

One adviser said no other central bank had launched such a test before and that it was  uncharted waters for both the BoE and banks .

David Strachan, a head of regulatory strategy at Deloitte, said of this year s assessment:  I think the way that it tests business models and capital levels 	. 	. 	. 	in terms of organisation and administration and logistics then it is more challenging this time around. 

Lee Thorpe, a manager at SAS UK and Ireland, an analytics company, added there would be more pressure on banks as a result of the new test.  A substantial change in approach to identify risks in the business model may require an alternative methodology, further challenging banks to deliver in tight timeframes,  he said.

The BoE also said on Monday that its Prudential Regulation Authority would launch a review into banks  lending standards in relation to consumer credit following soaring growth of the sector.

The review will focus on underwriting standards and the risk models employed by banks.

Consumer credit is one of the largest risks on a bank s balance sheet. Last year, UK banks had 19bn of impairments on credit cards, compared with 12bn on mortgages.

The BoE s Financial Policy Committee said it would also oversee banks  contingency plans to mitigate risks as the process for withdrawing from the EU unfolded. It said:  Risks to financial stability will be influenced by the orderliness of the adjustment to the new relationship between the United Kingdom and the European Union. 

The BoE warned a year ago, ahead of the EU referendum, that the vote represented the most significant near-term domestic risk to financial stability. However, Mark Carney, governor of the BoE, said earlier this year that this risk had abated.

The BoE is including for the first time an  anchor  on levels of severity used within its annual stress test. This means it will only get more severe in areas where the BoE thinks risks have increased.

The test, the results of which will be published in the fourth quarter, will examine how lenders deal with a rise in UK unemployment to 9.5 per cent   the same as last year   and a 33 per cent drop in house prices, slightly more than the previous year s scenario.

The bank is testing against a hypothetical 40 per cent decline in commercial real estate, compared with 42 per cent the previous year.

But the global economic scenario will be made more severe, with global gross domestic product contracting by 2.4 per cent and Chinese GDP by 1.2 per cent, implying that the BoE sees global economic hurdles as a greater threat.

Analysts at Citigroup said the  tougher global assumptions may have implications for HSBC s buyback programme 	. 	. 	. 	and the timeframe for StanChart to reinstate dividends. 

The changes are not designed to increase the overall severity of the test but rather show a shift in where the risks are expected to arise.

The BoE said:  In the annual cyclical stress test of the UK banking system, the sizes of the shocks to different sectors and economies are adjusted each year to deliver a similar stressed outcome.

Banking & Finance: State Street Tool Eases Social Investing

By Justin Baer 
422 words
27 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

The U.S. market in 2016 for investments that take into account environmental, social and governance risks was $8.7 trillion, according to the Forum for Sustainable and Responsible Investment. A Banking & Finance article Monday about State Street Corp.'s analytical tools for responsible investing incorrectly said the ESG market in the U.S. last year was $40 trillion.

(WSJ March 29, 2017)

(END)

Asset managers, pension funds and endowments will soon be able to screen their investments more easily for environmental, social and governance risks.

The development is thanks to State Street Corp., which is launching a new set of analytical tools aimed at capitalizing on the appetite for responsible investing. Many firms have made similar strides to help investors tackle what is called ESG exposure, but few can harness as much data as State Street, whose custody business safeguards $29 trillion.

The custody unit gives State Street a window into clients' entire portfolios, allowing them to offer ways to analyze reams of data on those holdings. That core custody business has produced steady, if underwhelming, profit margins, compelling State Street to hunt for new ways to tap into the data and potentially undiscovered investment ideas buried within.

At the same time, interest in ESG investing is surging. More investors are looking to ensure their holdings more closely reflect their values on everything from workforce diversity to limits on their carbon footprint. The overall market for ESG investments swelled to $40 trillion in U.S. assets under management last year, up 33% since 2014, according to the Forum for Sustainable and Responsible Investment.

"We have the root data, everything about the company itself," said Lou Maiuri, head of State Street's analytics and markets businesses. "We sit on 12% to 15% of the world's assets."

To help in its effort, the Boston-based bank has held discussions with more than 15 research firms, including MSCI Inc., Sustainalytics and TruValue Labs, to license their data. State Street plans to add those companies' research and ESG ratings alongside its existing risk-management and analytics systems for some 300 clients.

The plans follow efforts by State Street's money-management division, State Street Global Advisors, to add several exchange-traded funds and other offerings that invest in assets that screen stocks for environmental and social-justice criteria. The money manager said earlier this month it would push companies to put more women on their boards, a campaign punctuated by the placement of the "Fearless Girl" statue near Wall Street's bronze bull.

State Street formed its Global Exchange business in2013to showcase the bank'sanalytics. Some of the division's recent offerings include aprivate-equity index based on the data produced by its clientsand a quantitative-investment tool, called MediaStats, that tracks patterns in consumer behavior and media coverage.

Markets
Natural Gas Declines, Reversing Early Gains; Fears of oversupply keep rally in check

By Timothy Puko 
373 words
27 March 2017
20:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Natural-gas prices fell Monday, reversing early gains as fears of oversupply keep a rally in check.

Natural gas for April delivery settled down 2.4 cents, or 0.8%, at $3.052 a million British thermal units on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Prices rose as high as $3.124/mmBtu before flipping to losses late in the day.

A steady retreat began just after prices hit that high, prices unseen since Feb. 9. The market is range-bound now because of the prospects of low demand that traditionally comes after the end of winter-heating season and the prospects for more supply later this year, said Donald Morton, senior vice president at Herbert J. Sims & Co., who runs an energy-trading desk.

Supply currently is down 2% to 3% from a year ago, according to Platts Analytics, a unit of S&P Global Platts. That dropoff has help prices rise 10% month to date.

But the number of working gas rigs in the U.S. has nearly doubled from last year, according to oil-field services company Baker Hughes Inc. And the number of oil rigs, which also typically produce gas as a byproduct, has more than doubled, causing some to expect a wave of new natural gas across the country later this year.

"We have more rigs coming online and then more gas coming on to the market," Mr. Morton said. "I would not be surprised to see (prices) stuck here for the next 30 to 40 days."

Weather forecasts have also turned warmer, limiting the amount consumers may burn for heat in the weeks to come. Part of the recent rally came from heating demand that shrank storage levels from 21% above the five-year average in the week ended March 10 to below 15%. But that drastic reduction isn't likely to continue if weather turns much warmer than normal, Tim Evans, analyst at Citi Futures Perspective in New York, said in a note.

"The warmer than normal temperatures that follow will begin expanding the storage surplus again," Mr. Evans said.

Retailer Woes Depress Mall REITs --- Investor unease about broad shifts in the sector takes toll on real-estate shares

By Ben Eisen 
661 words
27 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Accelerating troubles among big retailers are weighing on a corner of the market for real-estate investment trusts, a rare bout of underperformance for one of the hottest sectors in recent years.

As Sears Holdings Corp. said Wednesday that it may struggle to continue operating, an index of regional mall REITs dropped 2.2%. Some individual REITs fared worse that day: CBL & Associates Properties Inc. fell 4.9%, Pennsylvania Real Estate Investment Trust dropped 3.5% and Washington Prime Group Inc. fell 3.3%.

All three stocks are down more than 17% this year, reflecting investor fears that store closures and financial struggles among the biggest tenants of shopping malls will hit the companies that finance mall real-estate assets.

Short interest in retail REITs is up about 20% from the end of last year, though it has fallen recently, according to S3 Partners, a financial-analytics firm. Investors who short a stock seek to profit from its decline.

"We believe some of the movements of these stock prices are warranted," said Laurel Durkay, associate portfolio manager of real-estate securities at Cohen & Steers. "We think we're in the third inning, with regard to store closures and the rationalization for real estate within the space."

REITs, which can hold real estate of all types, have been star performers in aggregate, boosted in recent years by the Federal Reserve's low-rate policies that made high-income holdings more attractive. The asset class returned nearly 11% annually between 2002 and 2016, the best performer among 10 investment groups tracked by J.P. Morgan Asset Management.

Last year, the S&P 500 added real estate as an 11th sector of the benchmark. With rates ticking higher this year, the sector is trailing the S&P's 4.7% rise in 2017; it is up just 1.9%.

The drop in mall REITs underscores investor unease about wider shifts in the retail sector as e-commerce sites like Amazon.com Inc. take market share from traditional brick-and-mortar stores. The SPDR S&P Retail exchange-traded fund has dropped 19% over the last two years, even as the S&P 500 climbed 12%.

Ms. Durkay said her firm has trimmed its mall-REIT exposure over the past year while adding to REITs that hold other assets, like data centers, particularly as companies accelerate online sales.

Some see the stock-price moves as an overreaction to a secular trend that has been ongoing. Sears is just one retailer to stoke concerns; GameStop Corp. said Thursday it would close 150 stores. Last month, J.C. Penney Co. said it would close as many as 140 of its 1,000 stores by June. Macy's Inc. said it would close nearly 70 stores. Sears said earlier this year it would shut a combined 150 Sears and Kmart stores.

"Retail REITs are getting oversold because malls are either dead or alive," said Scott Crowe, chief investment strategist at CenterSquare, which invests in real estate and infrastructure. "This has been a slow-moving train wreck for many years."

Many of the malls in REIT portfolios that have lost major tenants are finding they can replace those tenants, according to Calvin Schnure, senior economist at the National Association of REITs, a trade group.

He pointed to REIT data showing that among regional malls that have been open for more than a year, net operating income was up 3.4% in the final three months of last year from the same period a year earlier.

Still investors are largely in agreement that a good portion of the lower-tier malls will cease to exist in the coming years as their tenants continue to be pressured. Higher-tier malls, meanwhile, could be poised to benefit from consolidation in the sector.

Rate Rise Puts Squeeze on 0% Financing --- A go-to sweetener for many retailers and manufacturers gets more difficult to offer

By Serena Ng and Vipal Monga 
925 words
24 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B8
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

The Federal Reserve is pushing interest rates higher. Don't tell that to people who have become accustomed to buying everything at 0% financing.

Years of rock-bottom interest rates have led to a proliferation of no-interest financing offers for people looking to buy everything from cars to lawn mowers, jewelry and furniture. Manufacturers and retailers have come to lean heavily on these deals, which are an inducement for shoppers considering large or discretionary purchases.

Now, with interest rates climbing, the cost of these arrangements will rise, pinching profits at companies that derive a large chunk of their sales from shoppers who prefer to pay in bite-size pieces. Most retailers will likely absorb the higher costs to stay competitive because customers may turn elsewhere if they are asked to pony up interest charges.

The cost of providing 0% financing varies from company to company, but generally retailers pay a middleman -- usually a bank or finance company -- a few percentage points of a product's purchase price upfront. The practice is known as "buying down the rate to zero" because retailers are in effect footing the financing costs for their customers.

For a shopper buying a $10,000 hot tub with 0% financing over three years, that may translate into the retailer paying a bank or finance company around $1,000 upfront, or around 10% of the purchase price. The consumer then makes monthly payments to the finance company.

The upfront fees retailers pay are often tied to a short-term London interbank offered rate, which tends to rise in tandem with the Fed-guided federal-funds rate. As interest rates climb, banks are likely to increase these fees. The six-month Libor has risen to 1.43% from 0.9% a year ago, according to Bankrate.com, as the fed-funds rate has risen by half a percentage point.

The 0% deals will get more expensive, says Mike Rittler, head of retail card services at TD Bank, which provides no-interest financing to customers of 25 U.S. retailers, including sellers of furniture and tractors. Retailers could limit their costs by providing no-interest financing for shorter terms, he notes.

Companies in many cases say they don't plan to ditch the offers, which have become a cornerstone of their marketing efforts since the financial crisis.

But if profit margins get compressed, analysts say companies may be forced to act.

"Cash has been free for so long that everyone has been able to offer these no-interest deals," says David Bassuk, a managing director and co-head of the retail practice at consulting firm AlixPartners. "As it becomes more expensive for companies, the game is going to change."

In general, the longer the no-interest payment term, the higher the cost to the seller. "The offers that were the most generous will become harder to find, because retailers will have to give up more income to provide them," says Bill McCracken, CEO of Phoenix Synergistics, a consumer research firm.

So far, there are few signs that offers are fading. After all, interest rates, and most companies' funding costs, remain relatively low. Several retailers say shoppers also have come to expect the deals.

General Motors Co., which has long provided 0% financing for up to 72 months on many new car and truck models, expects to continue the deals. "The beauty of 0% is that it's pretty easy to understand." says Jim Cain, a GM spokesman.

In 2002, GM and other auto makers did away with 0% financing deals and moved to other types of sales incentives to woo customers. Roughly a year later, they brought back the deals to counter sluggish sales.

Television shopping network QVC last year started selling a $399 Dyson high-speed hair dryer that many people purchased using a six-month no-interest installment plan. "The product is a want and a desire, and not a need, and being able to pay in smaller amounts" makes the items more affordable for customers, says Peter Goodnough, QVC's vice president of customer insights and analytics.

The retailer has no plans to change its offers for no-interest financing.

At electronics and furniture chain P.C. Richard & Son, close to one-third of shoppers' purchases are made with store-branded credit cards that automatically let customers pay in interest-free installments, says Chief Financial Officer Tom Pohmer. Shoppers can get 12 to 60 months' financing on expensive items such as $2,000 mattresses. "The promotions are very important for our customers, and we will offer them when interest rates are high or low," Mr. Pohmer adds.

Although they acknowledge that rising interest rates will increase their costs, many retailers are hoping the Fed's rate increase is a sign of an improving economy, which should help their sales grow.

Consumers, meanwhile, can be very sensitive to changes in interest rates. Patrick Williams, senior director of marketing at Jacuzzi Group Worldwide, a Chino Hills, Calif., maker of hot tubs and other bath products, says his company has in the past experimented with 1.99% and 2.99% financing offers, with mixed results. "Consumers have become conditioned to seek out 0% financing," he says.

In recent years, credit-card issuers also have aggressively used 0% offers to persuade people to transfer their card balances over from rivals.

Authers  Note: Capital Performance

John Authers 
1,648 words
23 March 2017
00:22
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Sign up to receive Authers  Note daily by email "http://nbe.ft.com/nbe/profile.cfm?authersnote=Y"

Judging by the comments belowthe FT's Big Readfrom earlier this week on Capital Group, the huge California-based active fund manager, and its attempts to hold the line against the rising tide of passive management, many readers felt that it lacked a discussion of Capital's performance. I understand that concern, and my colleague Robin Wigglesworth also said in the comments that with hindsight he wished he had included a summary chart to show how Capital had done.

He also made the point, reasonably, that performance data for mutual funds is easily available at no cost. Readers could have looked this up for themselves.

For those who did not feel like doing so, I have spent some time at the Bloomberg terminal running some performance numbers for the Growth Fund of America, the flagship fund of Capital's American Funds group. The results are fascinating. My apologies, but I will have to present most of them as Bloomberg screen grabs.

For the sake of the exercise, I compared Growth Fund with SPY, the largest S&P 500 ETFand the most obvious passive competition these days, and Fidelity Magellan, which was for many years the largest, most famous and best-performing active mutual fund of them all. Its assets under management have fallen by almost 90 per cent since its heyday at the end of the 1990s, so it is a very interesting comparison with two funds that have been far more successful in recent years, in terms of pulling in money.

Over the past five years, Capital's Growth Fund is indeed ahead of the index, although not by much.

Well done, Capital Group. This is how the differing performance shows itself over time. I have not had the time to calculate Sharpe Ratios, but it is obvious by eyeballing the charts that there has been no significant difference in volatility for these funds over the past five years, which have by historical standards been very calm ones for the US stock market:

So Capital did do better, but they have done barely anything different than from tracking the index.

Over longer periods however, Capital comes out looking much better. We also begin to see the magic of compound interest. Over 24 years (the maximum for which Bloomberg had data), Capital's advantage compounded into huge extra returns:

This is what that looks like graphically:

Academic studies show frequently that repeated active outperformance over many years is hard to pull off, and that the number of active managers beating the index dwindles over time. This shows what you can do if you do manage to compound superior returns for a while.

Note, however, that almost all of this derives from one dramatic period, as the late 1990s bull market turned into a bubble, when Capital managed to handle the market far more adroitly than Magellan  or the index.

Having prudently decided to sit out the early stages of the final bull market phase, laggingbehind both Magellan and the index in 1996, it made two great decisions. First, it loaded up on growth stocks as the bubble came to a head, allowing it to outperform the market. And once at the top, it managed to shift into enough of the right stocks that its decline from there was no worse than that of the market. It is in thehopeof brilliant (or lucky) timing like this that people try an active manager rather than an index tracker.

It is no coincidence that Capital enjoyed a brief ascendancy as the largest mutual fund group, and Growth Fund of America took over as the largest mutual fund, in the period between the dotcom bust of 2000 and the credit crisis seven years later. It had handled the dotcom bust very well. How did it handle things when the top was reached in 2007? Nothing like as well. These numbers are for the five years starting from the S&P's top in October 2007:

Magellan evidently played that market break far worse, but the managers at Capital added no value to what their clients could have achieved by tracking the index. It is not surprising that since this incident, money has flowed ever more towards passive funds. Active funds (certainly not just Capital) failed to protect their investors during the most recent bear market.

Since the top in 2007, the similarity between Growth Fund and SPY is uncanny. Effectively, they have both given their investors exactly the same thing (SPY is very slightly ahead). It is just that Capital has paid more people and gone to much more trouble to come to exactly the same result:

This brings me to a defence of active investing that increasingly annoys me, because it is often stated without any evidence being offered to back it up. It is a popular contention that active is superior to passive because its managers can take evasive action in a downturn, while index trackers must blindly track the index downwards. Here is a choice comment from Robin's Big Read on Capital:

Bear in mind that I am generally known (fairly) to have been too negative about the US stock market as it has made its ascent. The choice between active and passive is not about asset allocation (I would not be too heavily in US equities, which will not seem bad in the event of a market break, but has not looked good of late), but about how to gain your exposure to equities. Almost everyone should always have at least exposure to equities.

Now, will I have reason to reproach myself for my enthusiasm for passive equity investing when the next equity downturn arrives? Here is how Growth Fund and Magellan performed, compared to the S&P 500 ETF, from the 2000 top to the 2002 bottom of the market:

OK, so Growth Fund did manage to outperform during that traumatic period, but by only 1.35 percentage points. Nobody could possibly look at the chart of that experience and conclude that Growth Fund of America had offered any kind of useful drawdown insurance:

Yes, again, Growth Fund outperformed the market very slightly. If you wanted to avoid a drawdown, it was hopeless: you needed to make a big asset allocation bet and do something else.

Note also that poor Magellan, which over many decades had earned the right to be regarded as the best actively managed fund of all time, underperformed the market in both of those drawdowns. And most active managers were more like Magellan than Growth Fund of America. Capital Group is indeed an excellent active management shop, but if it is operating a fund that has told investors it will stay fully invested in equities, then it is going to participate in any equity sell-off to the full.

This is all backward-looking but at least it is based in fact. Now let me try a little forward-looking analysis. Is there anything about Growth Fund's current portfolio that might incline me to believe that it will make money during the next big market downturn?

Going from public information, it is interesting to note first that its managers have made some great calls. All the FANG stocks (Facebook, Amazon, Netflix and Google) feature among its 10 largest holdings, as does Microsoft. Some 15.4 per cent of the fund is in the internet sector, its heaviest weighting.

Hats off to the managers for spotting so many of the winners of the past few years. Having done so, it is intriguing that they have not beaten the index by far more. But when it comes to the notion that this portfolio will offer you more protection in a downturn, I think that is ridiculous. We do not know how thenext downturn will play out, but I would expect the FANG stocks to hurt. And its holdings are so huge that it will be difficult to get out of them in a hurry and move on to something else. For example, its stake in Amazon, some 6 per cent of its portfolio, is worth some $8bn. 

And are these stocks particularly cheap? Bloomberg's analytics show that the top 10 holdings are on a price/earnings multiple of more than 60 times (which shows the limitations of p/e multiples), and a price/book multiple of more than three. Little drawdown insurance there.

As for asset allocation, it is not fully invested, having only 93.6 per cent in stocks. Only 1.5 per cent is in cash, as it has a small holding of bonds.In the event of another Lehman-type event, that should enable it to beat a tracker fund by a percentage point, maybe. By no leap of the imagination does it offer any insurance against another huge market sell-off.

So, this quick and cursory examination confirms that Growth Fund of America is an excellently run fund. It has done no worse than the index over the past decade, and anyone who has been in it for the very long term is still enjoying the compounded benefits from its adroit handling of the dotcom bubble. But I see no compelling reason to buy it rather than a passive tracker. It seems at present to be tracking the index while loading up on the hottest stocks of the moment. And we are, of course, talking about the very best that active management has to offer. As a general rule, minimising costs with a passive fund strikes me as the better idea.

And as for HoundDog's rather offensive notion that I will live to have a guilty conscience about recommending passive funds when the next downturns happens,I have only this to say to you:

Pro Cyber
Senate Panel Floats Ideas for Securing IoT

By Kate Fazzini 
610 words
23 March 2017
11:14
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A proliferation of internet-connected technologies, alongside emerging platforms like blockchain, artificial intelligence and quantum computing, have created new and yet-to-be-discovered risks that must be addressed, according to a March 22 hearing of the Senate Committee on Commerce, Science, and Transportation.

Senators questioned experts from Intel Corp., the venture capital firm Menlo Ventures, International Business Machines Corp., the Office of the Secretary of Defense and Cylance Inc. about how threats posed from new technologies can be met, sometimes by the new technologies themselves.

Some suggestions included using blockchain technology for safer, verifiable and anonymous information sharing between private sector firms, and establishing programs early that allow government and industry to anticipate the potential of quantum computers to quickly break existing encryption. A focus on the "shelf life" of internet-connected devices brought several suggestions, including a ratings system for security of those devices and consideration of a "sell by" date.

Focus on  IoT'

Several speakers questioned the feasibility of expecting private companies to protect against threats from military-grade attackers, particularly with the growing number of internet-connected devices belonging to the collective Internet of Things, or IoT. Committee chairman Sen. John Thune of South Dakota referenced a statistic that IoT devices will exceed 50 billion by 2020. Sen. Maggie Hassan of New Hampshire questioned whether and how private businesses could fend off IoT attacks from sophisticated adversaries.

Eric Rosenbach, former assistant secretary of defense for homeland security and global security, said that private companies can't be expected to defend themselves against a state-sponsored onslaught, and government intervention is often warranted. "Otherwise, the investment they would have to make in defending themselves would put them out of business," he said.

Venky Ganesan, a partner with Menlo Ventures, said there were promising solutions for de-risking IoT devices, and that he had been impressed with start-ups that provide company-level and individual product security "scores - similar to Standard and Poor's or Moody's," that could help businesses and consumers make more informed decisions about the security levels of various devices.

Steve Grobman, chief technology officer with Intel's security division, said his company had focused on ensuring IoT devices have "field repairability," in that devices are able to be accessed in the field in order to provide security updates. Sen. Richard Blumenthal of Connecticut suggested a recall process similar to that in place for products that cause physical hazards, while Caleb Barlow, vice president with threat intelligence at IBM, said the concept of an expiration date for devices could be considered.

Mr. Grobman said the issue of proliferation in the IoT marketplace raises some "very challenging questions."

"If a manufacturer sells a device for $30 with a three-year warranty, if a vulnerability is discovered in year seven, are they still subject to being required to deploy fixes? What about in cases where manufacturers no longer exist, and we are still left with millions of vulnerable devices?" said Mr. Grobman.

"I think one of the most important things that we can do in the near term is have consumers think about security in much of the same way that they think about reliability or safety in other products," said Mr. Grobman. "We really need to raise awareness that security in all devices is key."

(Kate Fazzini writes about cybersecurity for WSJ Pro. She has held roles in cybersecurity at Promontory Financial Group and JPMorgan Chase, and is an adjunct professor of cybersecurity at the University of Maryland, teaching cybersecurity for business and government. Write to Kate at kate.fazzini@dowjones.com.)

Finance & Markets: Chinese Group Pays Up for New York Tower

By Keiko Morris 
401 words
23 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B5
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

A Chinese conglomerate is in contract to buy a Park Avenue office building for $2.21 billion, among the highest prices ever paid for a New York tower, according to a person familiar with the deal.

HNA Group has entered into a deal to buy the 1.8 million square-foot skyscraper from sellers Brookfield Property Partners LP and the New York State Teachers' Retirement System, according to the person.

The agreement reflects the increasing allure of New York City real estate for major Chinese investors looking for property in so-called gateway cities in the U.S., said real-estate executives and analysts.

"For trophy-quality assets in New York City, particularly office buildings, there continues to be significant interest from Chinese investors who can write extraordinarily large checks and who, in all likelihood, are long-term holders," said Dustin Stolly, managing director of capital markets for real-estate services firm JLL. He wasn't involved in the deal.

The agreement was first reported by The Real Deal. Brookfield has a 51% stake in the tower, and its partner holds 49%.

Completed in 1967, the 45-story building at 245 Park Avenue has financial tenants including Societe Generale SA and Ares Capital Corp., and sits just north of Grand Central Terminal in the heart of Midtown Manhattan's traditional office corridor. Such properties are considered safe investments, said Ken McCarthy, principal economist at real estate services firm Cushman & Wakefield.

"It's more a focus on capital preservation than on the rate of return," Mr. McCarthy said. "There definitely has been a significant pickup in Chinese investment in properties in the U.S. overall and in New York City in particular."

In 2008, Boston Properties Inc. and investors, including the sovereign-wealth funds of Kuwait and Qatar, paid $2.8 billion to buy the General Motors Building at 767 Fifth Ave.

In 2014, Chinese investments in New York City commercial real estate totaled $500 million, according to Cushman & Wakefield and Real Capital Analytics Inc. It grew to $5.4 billion in 2015, the same year Anbang Insurance Group Co. bought the Waldorf Astoria hotel for $1.95 billion. Last year, Chinese investments in commercial real estate reached about $8 billion, representing about half of all foreign investment in the city.

Investment banks: America first

330 words
22 March 2017
00:04
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Two trends are obvious from the latest ranking of the world s largest investment banks: JPMorgan s climb to the top and Deutsche Bank s fall from grace. Expect this divergence to continue.

US banks are already in much better shape than their European rivals. Now they have the added advantage that they may be able to convince the new Washington administration to loosen strict domestic regulations. Rules burdensome to foreign lenders would probably stay in place.

The latest data from analytics company Coalition show that in all geographies and almost all sectors, JPMorgan already occupies first place. Only Morgan Stanley has managed to best it   outranking the bank in equities.

Meanwhile, Deutsche Bank is slipping down the league table. Across fixed income it has fallen from third place. In Asia, it has moved from top spot to third place   where it ties with three other banks. Jefferies, the US investment bank, has increased its fixed income revenue 48 per cent, which bodes well for those that have managed to hold on to the largest footprint in this segment. In the past year, the top three were all American.

Regulatory outperformance should give US banks a further boost. KBW estimates that removing extra buffers that domestic regulators have added to international rules could boost per share earnings 12 per cent next year.

Blanket Basel III capital requirements, for example, require a 3 per cent minimum leverage ratio, while US depository institutions must hold 5 per cent to be considered well capitalised. The excess can be returned to shareholders or invested into expanding balance sheets at the expense of European competitors.

The turning of the regulatory tide   if it happens   will come at exactly the right time for US institutions. It is in their interest to agree to regulatory standards high enough to keep European competitors at bay. Team Europe s loss will be financial stability s gain.

Octopus Ventures rides on AI success to raise 120m funding

Madhumita Murgia, European Technology Correspondent 
521 words
22 March 2017
04:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Octopus Ventures, the 10-year-old British venture capital firm that has backed three high-profile artificial intelligence start-ups acquired by US tech giants, has raised a fresh 120m tranche of funding to invest in new AI talent in the UK.

The 660m venture fund, which is the tech investing arm of UK fund management business Octopus Group, invested in natural language processing company Evi Technologies, predictive keyboard maker Swiftkey and AI video start-up Magic Pony, sold to Amazon, Microsoft and Twitter respectively.

Octopus has also backed several other successful British companies such as Lovefilm, which was also bought by Amazon, and property website Zoopla; but the three AI start-ups in its portfolio have led the firm to develop a specialism in such early-stage companies.

The industry could be extremely lucrative for the UK: Accenture, the consultancy, has estimated that AI could add up to 654bn to the national economy by 2035.

 Evi Technologies in Cambridge was sold to Amazon and now powers Alexa, the voice-activated speaker. We also backed Swiftkey which went to Microsoft last year [for $250m] and made a seed investment in Magic Pony, which went to Twitter [for $150m],  said Alex Macpherson, chief executive of Octopus Ventures.

 We have expertise in the machine-learning field, but the challenge today is pretty much every business that comes through to us is machine learning or artificial intelligence. 

Octopus is currently in discussions with two new British AI start-ups to provide seed funding from its fund over the coming months.

British AI skills have been particularly appealing to international companies, including the big US tech businesses such as Google, Apple and Uber.

In 2014, Google paid $400m for DeepMind, a London start-up that is now arguably a world leader in AI with a team of more than 250 academic experts in the fields of deep learning and machine intelligence.

A year later, Apple acquired UK-based speech technology start-up VocalIQ, and last December, Uber bought Geometric Intelligence, a partly Cambridge, UK-based AI start-up, to staff its new AI lab focused on transport.

It is not just the Americans looking to Britain to exploit AI expertise: late last year, Chinese private equity firm CSC Group funded an AI incubator in Britain, in a multimillion-pound partnership with British start-up incubator Founders Factory, led by entrepreneur and investor Brent Hoberman.

The goal was to invest in and grow five early-stage AI start-ups and launch two companies from scratch each year.  The UK has innovative artificial intelligence technology,  said Dania Zhou, senior managing director of CSC Group, who has been overseeing the deal.  We want to fuse the technology we find in the UK with the Chinese market. 

Ultimately, Octopus wants to build British companies that become world leaders in their own right. Mr Macpherson said:  Our whole concept is how can we help support British entrepreneurs to take their business the whole way, and not sell out to the Americans or others a bit too early.

GTCR Sells Cole-Parmer Instrument to Golden Gate

English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

GTCR sold Cole-Parmer Instrument Co., a maker of speciality lab equipment, to fellow private-equity firm Golden Gate Capital.

Cole-Parmer, of Vernon Hills, Ill., manufactures and distributes laboratory and industrial fluid-handling products, including chemicals, equipment, pumps, safety products and other items, to companies in the biotechnology, environmental, safety and pharmaceutical sectors.

GTCR acquired the business from Thermo Fisher Scientific Inc. in 2014. The Chicago firm bought the company for $480 million, according to a news release at that time.

GTCR's sale of Cole-Palmer is the result of a "narrow and focused process" that involved both private-equity firms and strategic companies, said Dean Mihas, a managing director at GTCR.

Goldman Sachs & Co. and Jefferies LLC advised Cole-Parmer on the transaction. J.P. Morgan Securities LLC advised GTCR. Kirkland & Ellis LLP and PriceWaterhouseCoopers LLP advised both Cole-Parmer and GTCR.

Mr. Mihas said GTCR helped carve out the company from Thermo Fisher in six months. In addition to stimulating organic growth, the firm helped Cole-Parmer complete six acquisitions over the course of its ownership. Mr. Mihas said that GTCR helped Cole-Parmer nearly double the earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of the business.

GTCR invests in the industries of financial services and technology; health care; growth business services; and technology, media and telecommunications.

The parent company of GTCR-backed public relations software firm Cison Inc. disclosed plans to go public by purchasing a publicly traded blank check company this week. Cision makes public relations software and analytics for companies to send out news releases, contact journalists and handle other campaigns.

Golden Gate has more than $15 billion in committed capital. The San Francisco firm targets six industry verticals: software; semiconductors and information technology hardware; retail and restaurants; industrials; IT and business services; and financial services.
