U.K. Venture Capitalists Fear Funding Drought Post Brexit; Funds backing fintech and startups could lose billions in financing

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine 
848 words
16 March 2017
14:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

BRUSSELS Venture capitalists fear they may be one of the financial sector's big losers from Brexit.

London is Europe's venture capital center but the funds backing the bloc's fintech and startup culture could lose billions of euros of funding once the U.K. officially leaves the European Union.

A main source of cash for VC funds in London comes from the European Investment Fund, an institution mainly backed by the EU that pumps money into venture capital funds and small nascent businesses.

The EIF has guaranteed a steady stream of funding over the past two decades to VC firms, 20% of which are based in the U.K.

According to Invest Europe, a lobby group representing venture capital and private-equity funds, the EIF committed  10.9 billion ($11.7 billion) to venture capital and small private-equity funds between 2012 and 2016. Of that amount,  2.8 billion, or nearly one quarter, went to the U.K. directly.

Venture capital is an important source of funding for startup and growing technology companies, many with high potential but facing higher risks with untested business ideas.

Unlike in the U.S., where the Harvard University endowment alone totals $35.7 billion and there are dozens of other endowments over $1 billion, Europe lacks the large-scale university endowments that invest in venture capital. Cambridge University's endowment, the largest in Europe, amounts to nearly 6 billion ($7.3 billion).

"European endowments and pension funds allocate assets to venture capital, but it is mostly narrowed to the largest two or three funds" said Christian Hernandez, a partner at venture-capital firm White Star Capital. "The role of the EIF has therefore been critical to fill this gap."

In 2015, the EIF provided U.K. startups with  655.8 million, which it estimated would generate an additional  2.87 billion in capital from other sources. At that point, it had supported 144 U.K. private-equity and venture funds and almost 30,000 small businesses, according to the EIF.

Such funding could disappear when the U.K. leaves the EU unless the U.K. strikes a special deal giving it access to EIF funds.

The loss of EIF cash would inflict pain on venture capital funds given the percentage of commitments to British-domiciled funds from the EIF, Mr. Hernandez said in an interview.

At the moment there is no clear guidance on what might replace EIF cash.

"The prospect of the U.K. no longer having those funds to invest creates a high level of uncertainty for the U.K. tech community," said Romilly Dennys, executive director of the Coalition for a Digital Economy, a lobby for U.K. digital and tech startups.

The lobbying group is pushing the U.K. government to seek continued collaboration with the EIF and advocates that the commercial arm of the state-owned British Business Bank becomes a stand-alone institution with expanded funding before Brexit.

But the British Business Bank is currently less equipped than its EU counterpart. EIF has a staff of 369 compared with 10 in the commercial arm of the British Business Bank, the U.K. lobbying group said.

Like other financial firms, venture capital managers in London are assessing the consequences of the U.K. leaving the single market, said Invest Europe Chief Executive Michael Collins. "For some, this may comprise relocating to Europe's other VC capitals, including Berlin and Helsinki, to ensure they will still have access to the money."

Mr. Collins said that while London's scale, combined with deep pools of talent and capital, isn't easy to replicate, there are other cities with thriving startup scenes that are attractive to venture capital funds.

Berlin, for example, is strong in digital media, Helsinki in mobile gaming and Paris in e-commerce. The Association for Financial Markets in Europe, which represents firms in wholesale financial markets, said in a March report that the EU VC industry is concentrated around 10 members of the bloc.

Moreover, most VC firms employ small numbers compared with banks and aren't trapped in long-term leases, making staff moves less onerous.

White Star Capital, which invests in the tech industry, for instance, is based out of London's "Second Home," a workspace in London's hip Brick Lane neighborhood that boasts original Bauhaus furniture and weekly yoga classes.

While the loss of EIF funding could lead to a stronger VC presence in Paris, Berlin, and the Nordics, Mr. Hernandez said tax incentives and a tech ecosystem boosted by a handful of universities focusing on artificial intelligence and machine learning give the U.K.'s VC scene an advantage.

"While I believe the uncertainty of Brexit might slow it down and allow others to benefit," said Mr. Hernandez, "I am hopeful that the government will realize the asset the digital economy has been thus far and continue to invest in incentives that support funding."

Pro Private Markets
Cooper's Take: Making an Orderly Exit; Study finds many firms reactive in making exits and that data and tax planning could lead to a more orderly departure

By Laura Cooper 
446 words
16 March 2017
11:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

For private-equity players planning an exit, better data and tax planning could help create a smoother exit process and maximize returns, according to EY's Private Equity Global Divestment Study 2017.

Bill Stoffel, the EY US Private Equity leader, said his firm has seen more work assisting private-equity firms with selling their businesses, a change from five years ago when they had only a few of these projects.

The Private Equity Global Divestment Study found 56% of those surveyed including private-equity executives across the Americas, Asia-Pacific region, Europe, the Middle East and Africa said they sometimes have an exit plan, while 30% said they never use them.

The study found many private-equity firms leave exit strategies to deal teams rather than to internal committees focused on planning for exits. Many private-equity firms are often reactive often responding to inbound interest in their portfolio companies and as a result, some value may be left on the table through a "poorly timed" exit.

According to the study, 86% of the respondents said they don't have a systematic playbook for an exit, while 47% of respondents said their firm's last exit was "too early or too late, and value was sacrificed as a result."

Although there has been much uncertainty surrounding the Donald Trump administration, many in the financial community believe he has improved the climate for doing business. Mr. Stoffel said the past few years have seen a consistent volume of exit activity and that "from an overall perspective, people are bullish on what [a Trump presidency] could mean for business."

Mr. Stoffel said private-equity players could benefit from using the data it receives from portfolio companies including customer volume and other internal data to support the growth story of a company that is on the way out. Predictive analytics, Mr. Stoffel said, is something that could benefit sellers because it often takes a while for data to play out in real time. The challenge, he said, is dealing with poor quality data that can be transferred from portfolio companies to private-equity firms.

In addition to predictive data, Mr. Stoffel said tax planning for an exit is crucial to a well-executed exit.

"There's real tax planning that has to go on to maximize an exit," he said. "It's really understanding your potential buyers and what attributes they would pay up for."

Deng's Take: China's Regulators Struggle With Risky Investment Products

China's Regulators Struggle With Risky Investment Products

China's financial regulators are trying to put their heads together in dealing with a booming asset-management sector. They have a long way to go.

Investment products have run amok here, coming from banks, securities companies, trust firms, insurers and their subsidiaries. The value of country's asset-management sector totaled around 60 trillion yuan ($8.7 trillion at current exchange rates) as of June 2016, approaching the previous year's gross-domestic-product level, according to the China Securities Regulatory Commission. The tally nets out overcounting due to funds investing in other funds.

Much of the risk can be found at banks, which hold trillions of U.S. dollars' worth of investment products off their balance sheets products that fly under the regulatory radar. Wealth-management products from banks made up more than 40% of total assets under management by asset managers, followed by products from trust firms and mutual funds, according to the securities regulator. The products fall under the scope of a different financial regulator depending on the issuer.

China's central bank and the securities, banking and insurance regulators have drafted a set of rules for asset managers, aimed at streamlining oversight. If the plan is implemented, fund managers would have to set aside reserves, cap leverage in their products and rein in the risk of assets they invest in; they wouldn't be able to guarantee returns. The draft still needs to be approved by the State Council, China's cabinet.

But already, there's a good dose of skepticism about its implementation, as well as how effective the rules would be.

"History shows that Chinese financial institutions are extremely innovative," said Chen Long, an economist with research firm Gavekal Dragonomics. "I would not exclude the possibility that they would come up with something more innovative that dodges regulations."

Last year, China's top leaders "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-takes-aim-at-regulatory-overhaul-1468507534" to address regulatory loopholes that allowed the rise of risky investment products. There was talk of putting the People's Bank of China in charge of the other financial regulators but nothing has materialized since. The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-shakes-up-top-economic-team-ahead-of-major-power-shuffle-1487840024" to head the China Banking Regulatory Commission has fueled expectations that China might merge its banking and insurance regulators, handing more power to Mr. Guo. At his first press conference as head banking regulator earlier this month, however, Mr. Guo played down that possibility, calling it a rumor.

Most analysts aren't hopeful that Chinese leaders will make a decision soon on how to proceed. President Xi Jinping transitions to a second term this fall, making it possible that any regulatory overhaul would drag out to next year, some say.

Without greater collaboration between regulators, financial risks from China's asset-management sector continue to loom large.

"It seems there are more signs of coordination over the past six months," Mr. Chen said. "But when a merger is going to happen is anyone's guess."

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

BOE Deputy Governor Hogg Resigns After Critical Report

By Jason Douglas

LONDON A senior Bank of England official resigned after a panel of lawmakers said her failure to disclose a potential conflict of interest meant she lacked the professional competence to do her job.

Charlotte Hogg, who was appointed chief operating officer at the BOE in 2013 before being named deputy governor for markets and banking in February, quit on Monday, the BOE said Tuesday.

Her resignation came after parliament's treasury committee said Ms. Hogg's failure to disclose that her brother worked in a senior role at U.K. lender Barclays PLC during almost four years at the bank represented "a serious error of judgment."

"The committee considers that her professional competence falls short of the very high standards required to fulfill the additional responsibilities of deputy governor for markets and banking," the panel said in a report into her appointment as deputy governor made public Tuesday.

The resignation of a senior official after such unusually harsh criticism is unprecedented. The panel said the episode raises wider concerns over the governance of the 322-year-old central bank, which faces multiple policy challenges as the U.K. heads toward its departure from the European Union.

Prime Minister Theresa May won parliamentary votes Monday that clear the way for her to begin two-year exit talks before the end of March.

For Ms. Hogg, her resignation marks a low point in a long and successful career in finance that took her from the BOE to senior positions at banks including the U.K. unit of Banco Santander SA before her return to the central bank almost four years ago alongside Mark Carney, the institution's Canadian governor.

Mr. Carney handpicked Ms. Hogg to handle the day-to-day management of the central bank to leave him and other top officials to focus on policy. In a statement, Mr. Carney said he deeply regrets Ms. Hogg's resignation but respects her decision, which he said was taken with the best interests of the BOE in mind. "The Bank of England today is stronger, more diverse, secure and effective in large part because of Charlotte Hogg," he said.

In its report, parliament's treasury committee said Ms. Hogg's failure to disclose that her brother was director of group strategy at Barclays breached the BOE's code of conduct for senior staff guidelines that Ms. Hogg had herself helped draft.

It dismissed Ms. Hogg's assertion, given in written evidence to the committee this month, that as chief operating officer a potential conflict of interest never arose with regards to her brother's work. "The committee considers that such a potential, actual or perceived conflict could well arise," it said, given that Ms. Hogg served on the BOE's board of directors, known as its court.

The BOE is expected to hold its benchmark interest rate steady Thursday following officials' March policy meeting.

Thomas Vartanian in Running to Be Fed Vice Chair for Supervision

By Kate Davidson and Nick Timiraos

A lawyer who has represented shareholders and financial firms in lawsuits against the government is under consideration for a top financial regulatory post at the Federal Reserve, according to two people familiar with the matter.

Thomas Vartanian, a partner in the law firm Dechert LLP, is on the shortlist to become the Fed's vice chairman for supervision, the people said.

Mr. Vartanian couldn't immediately be reached for comment.

The position was created by the 2010 Dodd-Frank law to oversee the largest U.S. banks, but President Barack Obama never nominated anyone to the job. Fed governor Daniel Tarullo, who has served as the de facto vice chairman for supervision, will step down in April, leaving three vacancies on the Fed board.

Officials in the Trump administration have said filling the vice chairman's job is a priority and have broadened their search for the job in recent weeks. A White House spokeswoman declined to comment.

A leading contender for the position, GE Capital executive David Nason, took himself out of the running earlier this month.

Mr. Vartanian has worked as a financial regulatory attorney in Washington for years, representing banks in complex financial transactions including failures, conservatorships and enforcement challenges. He filed a legal brief on behalf of the U.S. Chamber of Commerce supporting MetLife Inc. in its legal battle over the decision by a council of regulators to subject the insurer to federal oversight.

Mr. Vartanian represented hedge-fund firm Paulson & Co. when it invested in the purchase of the failed thrift IndyMac Bank by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. in 2009. Steven Mnuchin, who is now serving as Treasury secretary, oversaw the turnaround of IndyMac, later rebranded OneWest Bank. Mr. Vartanian also represented investors in the sale of BankUnited, which was purchased from the FDIC by a private equity group that included Wilbur Ross, who is now the commerce secretary.

Mr. Vartanian also served as general counsel of the Federal Home Loan Bank Board and chief legal officer of the Federal Savings and Loan Insurance Corp. during the Reagan administration, according to a bio on his firm's website. Before that, he served in the legal division of the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency, another big bank regulator.

WSJ Pro: FDIC's Hoenig Details Idea for a New Glass-Steagall Law

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTON Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. Vice Chairman Thomas Hoenig proposed a plan Monday that would require banks to separate their commercial and investment activities and hold more capital in exchange for simplified regulatory requirements that could reduce compliance burdens.

In a speech at the Institute of International Bankers annual conference, Mr. Hoenig detailed a proposal for large bank holding companies to separately capitalize and manage their traditional commercial banking activities and their nontraditional activities, such as investment banking.

That aspect of Mr. Hoenig's proposal closely resembles the Glass-Steagall Act, a Depression-era law that was repealed in 1999. Mr. Hoenig has long praised Glass-Steagall and has been a vocal proponent of breaking up big banks.

While on the campaign trail, President Donald Trump repeatedly called for a "21st century Glass-Steagall Act," an idea that White House press secretary Sean Spicer reiterated last week.

Mr. Hoenig has been under consideration for several top economic posts in the Trump administration, and he has reportedly expressed interest in a position like Federal Reserve vice chair of supervision or chairman of the FDIC. Nominations for these positions haven't been announced.

"Through the booming 20th century and during the era of Glass-Steagall, investment banks underwrote companies that helped to build the modern economy and became part of the national fabric," Mr. Hoenig said Monday. "These recommendations...will provide a more competitive playing field among and between commercial and investment banks."

As part of the separation, banks would "get relief from the Volcker rule" on speculative investments, Mr. Hoenig said. While banks would still be prohibited from engaging in proprietary trading, "the separately managed and capitalized affiliates would be able to do so with proper controls in place and assurances that the safety net was appropriately confined and risk adequately capitalized."

Parts of the proposal dovetail with similar regulatory-overhaul efforts on Capitol Hill. Mr. Hoenig proposes, as he has previously, a 10% capital threshold for banks while removing risk-based capital and liquidity requirements, which he has criticized as arbitrary and burdensome.

The same 10% level has also been proposed by House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling, who is expected to release broad regulatory overhaul legislation in the coming weeks.

Currently, U.S. banks are required to meet a 6% leverage ratio, higher than the 3% buffer required by the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision. Basel Committee guidelines aren't binding and it is up to individual countries to set their own regulatory requirements.

Mr. Hoenig pointed to simpler requirements and higher capital threshold as a boon to regional and community banks. Most community banks already meet a 10% capital threshold.

Mr. Hensarling's legislation doesn't include the Glass-Steagall-like elements in Mr. Hoenig's proposal.

WSJ Pro: Don't Expect Total Repeal of Obama Financial Regulation, Top Lawyer Says

By Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON The Trump administration is likely to take a more limited approach to financial deregulation than what many in the industry were hoping for, a top Wall Street lawyer said Monday.

H. Rodgin Cohen, senior chairman of law firm Sullivan & Cromwell LLP, told the Institute of International Bankers annual conference not to expect a total repeal of Obama-era regulations, playing down the bank-stock surge following President Donald Trump's election.

The new administration's "approach to deregulation won't be necessarily all inclusive or uniform and it's likely to focus on smaller and midsize banks," Mr. Cohen said. "Accordingly, I think the focus will be more on repealing or revising the existing regulations as opposed to shaping or blocking new regulations."

Mr. Cohen pointed to three pending regulations the Trump administration is likely to block entirely. One is the Labor Department's "fiduciary rule" restricting compensation for retirement advice, which President Trump delayed through an executive order in February. Other pending rules likely to be blocked are a regulatory proposal that restricts certain incentive-based compensation for financial-company executives and stricter liquidity requirements, known as the net stable funding ratio.

In terms of modifying existing rules, Mr. Cohen said revisions to the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act could include relieving some of the regulatory requirements on banks' liquidity coverage, easing the capital requirements portion of the Federal Reserve's annual stress tests, and changing the Volcker rule banning certain types of bank trading and investing.

Some Republicans have called for modifying other areas of Dodd-Frank, including the "orderly liquidation authority" given to federal regulators to wind down a failed firm in an emergency situation. Mr. Cohen said the idea was troubling. A "repeal could increase the economies of exposure and the event of a future financial crisis &and encourage more stringent international regulation of the largest banks," he said.

If the GOP proposes legislative changes to Dodd-Frank that can garner some Democratic support, "there is a realistic prospect that we will have some banking legislation within the next 18 months," he said.

However, any change in the supervision of banks won't happen until Mr. Trump nominates and Congress confirms the appointees for top regulatory agencies such as the Fed and the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency.

Comptroller of the Currency Thomas Curry, speaking later Monday at the same conference, stressed the importance of maintaining heightened supervision.

"Some will suggest easing safeguards. A few will even suggest eliminating them," Mr. Curry said in prepared remarks. "We must not forget the lessons of the past crisis. There is exceptional value in strong supervision value that, regrettably, sometimes only becomes clear in its absence. We cannot let our guard down."

Mr. Cohen said there is a "sleeper, and potentially momentous, legislative proposal" hinted at in Mr. Trump's financial executive orders last month the idea of consolidating the three primary bank regulators: the Fed, the OCC and the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.

At the very least, Mr. Cohen said he was pushing for the regulators to harmonize their approach to enforcing cybersecurity.

"Even if we can't get to consolidation generally, what we desperately need to do now is have consolidation of their cyber areas so that we have uniform cyber review of this exponential threat to the banking industry," he said.

Many regulators say they have significantly ramped up their cybersecurity supervision. Mr. Curry said the OCC has collaborated with international regulators to enhance systems for defending against cyberattacks, including launching a school for anti-money-laundering training with foreign supervisors.

"Succeeding in the fight against cyber risks requires international collaboration, and open communication across our borders, so that our defenses are equally strong," Mr. Curry said.

North Korean Banks Under U.S. Sanctions Remain on Swift Network

By Katy Burne

At least four North Korean banks under U.S. Treasury sanctions remain on the Swift money-transfer messaging network, even after the network deactivated a handful of the country's banks sanctioned by the United Nations the latest sign a squeeze on Pyongyang has failed to completely shut off the regime's access to the global banking system.

The U.S. Treasury-sanctioned banks that remain on Swift include the state-owned Foreign Trade Bank of the Democratic People's Republic of Korea, the country's primary foreign-exchange bank; Kumgang Bank; Koryo Credit Development Bank; and North East Asia Bank, according to people familiar with the network. A search on Swift's website listed active bank identifier codes for the institutions as of Monday.

The U.S. designated for sanctions the Foreign Trade Bank in 2013, saying it facilitated weapons of mass destruction programs in North Korea. The other three were sanctioned in December as the U.S. targeted entities it said supported the North Korean government and its weapons programs following the Asian nation's September 2016 nuclear test.

The apparent sanctions gap raises questions about how easily North Korea could move currency through alternative banking channels, something the U.N. said it has been known to do in the past through fronting companies.

Calls to the permanent mission of North Korea to the U.N. weren't returned, and attempts to reach the banks were unsuccessful.

Swift, formerly known as the Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication, doesn't itself move money between customers and countries. It is a member-owned cooperative operating under European Union law that provides the messaging platform necessary for users to order payments to one another.

While based in Brussels and regulated by Belgian authorities, the company intersects daily with U.S. financial institutions, processing tens of millions of payment instructions, including through a large facility in Culpeper County, Va.

But Swift, because of its European base, has been more responsive to EU sanctions than U.S. ones, a Treasury official said. Companies in the EU, including Swift, are required by law to comply with U.N. sanctions. But Swift would have to comply with U.S. law, including U.S. sanctions on North Korea, only where its activities fall under U.S. jurisdiction, the Treasury official said. Nothing in the U.S. sanctions regime prohibits Swift's activities when the company operates completely outside of the U.S.

If a European bank traded with a sanctioned North Korean bank using a U.S. intermediary or correspondent bank, the U.S. firm would have a legal obligation to block the flow of funds, the Treasury official said.

Swift said in a statement that "any decision to lift or impose sanctions on countries or individual entities rests solely with the competent government bodies and legislators." The company said it had a legal requirement to comply with EU regulations, being incorporated under Belgian law.

Swift's lead regulator, the National Bank of Belgium, said sanctions were within the remit of the Belgian government, not the central bank, and had no further comment regarding U.S.-sanctioned North Korean banks still using the network. Representatives for Belgium's Ministry of Finance didn't respond to requests for comment.

As a general rule, Swift doesn't block service to a customer or country, although its bylaws say that it reserves the right to terminate a user if it hurts the company's "reputation, brand or goodwill." Last year, it threatened to remove users for material security breaches.

In 2012, when Swift disconnected a group of Iranian banks for sanction purposes, it was because the EU wrote a law prohibiting financial messaging to those firms. Iran's Swift traffic was robust at the time, but activity from North Korea ranks among the lowest in the more than 220 countries on the network. Some said the impact of disconnections would become clearer in time.

"It may start to encourage these rogue regimes to figure out alternative ways to move money," said Leonard Schrank, who was chief executive of Swift for 15 years until 2007.

Swift had been servicing the U.N.-sanctioned banks because the Belgian government was providing authorizations for it to receive fees from those firms, as long as they met certain criteria and a transaction limit wasn't surpassed.

A Feb. 27 report by a U.N. panel of experts called attention to the activity. The Belgium Ministry of Finance rescinded the authorizations, and Swift said it promptly delisted three of the seven North Korean firms under U.N. sanctions: Bank of East Land, Korea Daesong Bank and Korea Kwangson Banking Corp.

Another four North Korean banks previously volunteered to be removed from the Swift network: Amroggang Development Banking Corp., Daedong Credit Bank, Tanchon Commercial Bank and Korea United Development Bank.

"Swift seeks, and has always sought, to cooperate fully and transparently with its competent authorities," the financial messaging company said. "Swift neither receives fees from, nor makes payments to, designated entities in the absence of these authorizations."

The issue of the remaining banks on Swift that fall under U.S. sanctions could be resolved with diplomatic initiatives between the U.S. and the EU. But some in Congress are considering legislative options for cracking down on Swift for conducting business with North Korean firms. Last week, Sen. Cory Gardner (R., Colo.) urged Swift to revoke all North Korean banking privileges.

In 2012, a legislative amendment was approved to provide the U.S. with authority to sanction Swift if it facilitates financial transactions with Iran.

Brian Kaveney, a spokesman for Rep. Ted Yoho (R., Fla.), chairman of the Foreign Affairs Subcommittee on Asia and the Pacific, said his office was organizing a March 21 hearing where one component was expected to focus on Swift and its role in offering financial tools to North Korea.

J.P. Morgan Moves Ahead With Plan to Drop Commissions in IRAs

By Michael Wursthorn

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. is moving ahead with its plan to drop commissions in retirement accounts that use a financial adviser, the latest brokerage to push forward with efforts to comply with an Obama-era retirement rule that is under review for repeal or revision.

The New York bank told some wealth-management customers with individual retirement accounts that as of April 7 their "financial adviser will no longer be able to provide investment guidance," according to a letter sent to clients and viewed by The Wall Street Journal. Affected clients will be automatically moved to a self-directed retirement account on that date, the letter added.

About 5% of J.P. Morgan's $1.1 trillion in wealth-management assets are held in retirement accounts, and only those that are charged commissions are affected by the change, said a bank spokesman who also confirmed the letter.

J.P. Morgan said in its letter "it may not proceed with this transition" of accounts if the new regulation requiring brokers to act in the best interest of retirement savers isn't implemented as planned.

The regulation known as the fiduciary rule and expected to affect some $3 trillion of retirement assets in the U.S. has been thrown into limbo since the Labor Department proposed delaying its April 10 implementation date by 60 days. That proposal came after President Donald Trump signed an executive action in February ordering the Labor Department to conduct a comprehensive review of the rule and its effects.

Proponents of the rule sought to eliminate conflicts from investment advice that has been estimated to cost American families $17 billion a year and pushes down annual returns on retirement savings by a percentage point. Many financial-industry leaders have said those figures are inflated and have fought against the regulation, arguing that it would cut small retirement savers off from advice and would be a costly burden for brokerages to implement.

J.P. Morgan said in November that it would stop accepting commissions in retirement accounts in an effort to avoid the more-stringent compliance standards required under the rule. The bank said affected retirement clients in its Chase Wealth Management, Private Bank and J.P. Morgan Securities units would be steered toward accounts where they manage the investments themselves or accounts that charge fees based on a percentage of assets, which could be costlier for those who trade little.

Most of J.P. Morgan's clients in recent months were given the choice of moving to a fee-based account or a self-directed option, the bank's spokesman said. Those who elected to move to an account where they manage their own investments received letters recently, the spokesman said, while others who haven't made a decision were also issued the notice.

The bank followed Merrill Lynch last fall when it announced it would abandon Wall Street's traditional sales model of charging commissions whenever retirement investors who worked with a broker bought or sold stocks, bonds, mutual funds and other investment products. Merrill is now considering relaxing its plan.

Wells Fargo Official Says Cyber Investments Pointing Way to Better Risk Management

By Steven Norton

Wells Fargo & Co.'s chief information security officer says cybersecurity investments in recent years have reduced overall risk and provided the bank with the foundational infrastructure it needs to begin managing that risk in a more preemptive and proactive way.

Now the bank is working to apply those security capabilities available across business units to improve risk-based decisionmaking.

"Investments made to enhance the program have helped reduce risk to a level that good, sound risk management or return-on-investment decisions can be made in the future," said Rich Baich, the bank's chief information security officer since 2012.

The bank views cyber risk in much the same way it views broader business risk. Mr. Baich reports to Wells Fargo's head of operational risk and compliance and the CISO job "is much more risk management than technical security," he said.

Far beyond securing laptops and mobile devices, broader risk management has become a strategic imperative inside the CISO's office. "We think about cybersecurity in the context of risk," John Hering, co-founder and executive chairman of Lookout Inc., said last month during the CIO Network conference in San Francisco.

In 2015, McDonald's Corp. CISO Marc Varner said risk management had become one of the most interesting parts of his job. "Any idiot can secure a computer; just throw it in the ocean or something," he said. "The trick is  & can you help your enterprise manage risk, and that balance between risk and reward."

Wells Fargo's Mr. Baich said the most important metric he tracks is a cyberattack's probability of occurrence. "That's probably the one thing in the risk formula you can change," he said. Seeing other organizations fall victim to a particular attack might increase the probability that hackers are using similar techniques, indicating the bank should take greater steps to protect against them.

Wells Fargo uses a number of tools to identify potential vulnerabilities and measure the probability of occurrence. Increasingly the bank must be able to look at multiple threat indicators and determine whether they point to different or larger vulnerabilities. "It is not a discrete science whatsoever," Mr. Baich said.

He laid out a three-pronged strategy for boosting the bank's cybersecurity: bolster the foundation, make that foundation operational, then optimize those operations.

A number of investments have set the stage for what he called "cyber correlation automation," essentially the ability to collect and digest large amounts of data from many different sources to quickly identify, predict and respond to attacks. "It's really big data," Mr. Baich said. "That's what some of these investments have allowed you to do. Do you know what you need, can you get it, and can you use it."

Next, Mr. Baich plans to work with groups across the bank, such as fraud and corporate investigations, to help them use those correlation capabilities to enhance their own controls.

"In essence it's turning [cybersecurity] into enablement," Mr. Baich said. That means getting the right information to the right decisionmakers quickly so they can improve risk-based decisionmaking and enhance internal controls.

Beijing vows to clean up digital small lenders

Gabriel Wildau in Shanghai 
800 words
13 March 2017
21:02
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Chinese authorities are preparing new rules to reduce risks caused by the rapid growth of online small-loan companies, which have emerged as among the most active lenders in the country following a crackdown on peer-to-peer rivals.

Chinese companies including Wanda, Ctrip, and LeEco have launched small-loan units over the past year. Earlier this month, JD.com said it was spinning off JD Finance, while retaining a share of profits in a deal that valued the lender at Rmb50bn ($7.2bn), mirroring Alibaba s spin-off of Ant Financial.

While the government has looked favourably on large companies with troves of proprietary data to expand into lending, regulators are growing concerned about a fleet of upstarts with little expertise in risk control. The regulator is considering ways to curb excesses in the small-loans market.

 We ve noticed that some so-called  internet small lenders  or  online small lenders  fundamentally lack a real customer base or real internet technology or big data. They just put out their name and blindly launch their business,  Li Junfeng, director of the financial inclusion department at the China Banking Regulatory Commission, told the small-loan industry association recently.

Loans outstanding among China s 8,673 small-loan companies totalled Rmb927bn at the end of last year, according to government data. That is more than the Rmb673bn outstanding from peer-to-peer lenders, according to Online Lending House.

But even that figure understates the true scale of activity by small-loan companies because these groups are among the most active issuers in China s burgeoning securitisation market. Securitisation of small-loan assets hit Rmb82bn in 2016, up from Rmb13bn a year earlier, according to data from Wind Info.

 These large companies want to cultivate new profit sources, and online small loans are a natural entry point,  says Wang Haimei, analyst at Splendid Profit, a fintech consultancy in Shanghai.  By lending upstream and downstream in their industry chains, they can consolidate their business relationships. 

Dalian Wanda Group   the mall developer that has expanded into movie production, sports and amusement parks   launched a lending app last July. Wanda Online Loans offers collateral-free loans to Wanda s smaller shopping-mall tenants, with loan approval available in as little as one day. Wanda also offers consumer loans with credit assessments based on customer data from Wanda's hotels, movie theaters, and ecommerce platforms.

Ctrip, the online travel agency, offers loans to Chinese tourists who must show proof of a minimum level of bank deposits in order to obtain travel visas. Haier, the home appliance maker, offers loans to online appliance retailers and street-side mobile phone shops.

Mr Li said the CBRC is preparing new regulations to address rising risks in the industry and advised provincial authorities to exercise caution in approving new online lenders in the meantime.

Traditional, offline small-loan companies are restricted to lending within their home provinces where they know local businesses. But provincial governments have approved a new wave of online small-loan companies in recent years. These new online groups are not subject to regional restrictions, opening the door to new risks.

Fenqile, whose name translates as  Installment Payments Joy , targets students with an army of 50,000 part time sales staff at 3,000 universities. Its website acts as an ecommerce platform that allows customers to buy cell phones, clothes, or holiday travel on credit, even without credit history or a parent s permission. Its slogan is  Love what I love, don t wait . The company declined an interview request.

Online small-loan approvals have become a valuable commodity amid aggressive enforcement ofnew rules on P2P lending announced last year. The rules cap P2P loans at Rmb200,000 for an individual and Rmb1m for a company. They also forbid peer-to-peer lenders from operating  fund pools  that allow platforms to fund payouts on maturing investment products with inflows from new product sales.

The goal of the ban on fund pools is to force P2P groups to serve as pure intermediaries that match investors with loans. Yet fund pools have been crucial to enabling these platforms to offer products with both short maturities and high yields. By contrast, small-loan companies rely on their own capital to fund loans, which means they have greater freedom to manage liquidity.

 Without fund pools, it s unlikely that peer-to-peer companies can be profitable, but online small loan companies don't have this kind of a limit,  says David Wu, executive director for financial service risk at EY in Shanghai.  In China, once the government starts limiting approval of something, it becomes a scarce resource. The message is very clear: this is worth something.

Regulatory easing will not kill off financial risk model makers

Laura Noonan 
789 words
13 March 2017
04:44
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

There are plenty of bankers who would applaud if Donald Trump s drive to ease global banking regulations reduces the influence of the risk managers, a group that has grown more powerful since the financial crisis.

After all, these are the risk managers who sucked up hundreds of millions of dollars of their banks  annual budgets to pay for obscure models and arduous compliance processes at a time when lenders were battling through a crisis and investment was in short supply.

These are the risk managers who installed themselves in the middle of banks  trading floors and critical processes so they could monitor exactly what was going on and prevent any potential problems before they blossomed   but ended up putting the brakes on profitable and safe trades too.

And these are the risk managers who created the often-tedious compliance programmes that bank employees must undergo regularly or face the consequences such as   in some institutions   losing their bonuses.

But risk managers will remain a force to be reckoned with for years to come, according to bank executives and industry experts. They say that even with Mr Trump s vow to row back the Dodd-Frank Act that heaped regulation on banks, and even with the failure of global policymakers to reach agreement on the next Basel capital measures, there are plenty of other factors that mean risk management will remain a priority.

There is ample space too for developers of complex models   tools that help assess the risk involved in a given activity   that have proliferated since the crisis. This is in spite of the fact that such models are expected to become less important under new capital rules that give banks less flexibility in how they assess the risks of their loan books.

 We believe there is also economic benefit of applying these models to our portfolios and positions,  says Christian Bluhm, chief risk officer at UBS.  We are not doing this just for the regulators, but also for ourselves as a bank. 

Peter Hahn, professor at the London Institute of Banking and Finance, says models were largely invented for mortgages, credit cards and other consumer loans and they have been very useful for banks when they make decisions about which customers they take on.

Since models were first built, regulations have rewarded banks that can show their portfolios are less risky. This has tempted risk managers to come up with models that show their banks require less capital and can therefore report better earnings, says Mr Hahn.

Under new capital rules, banks expect to have much less flexibility to use models to reduce the risk weighting of mortgages and other loans, and in turn reduce the amount of capital those loans demand. This could lead to less investment in some areas of risk management.

But bankers stress that some aspects of the changing regulatory environment will require more modelling, not less. The fundamental review of the trading book   a Basel initiative that deals with how much capital banks must hold for the stocks and bonds they trade with clients, which is due to come into force in 2019   heavily incentivises banks to use models to calculate the risks from changing market prices.  It s pretty clear that all banks will heavily invest in risk models,  says Mr Bluhm, describing the fundamental review of the trading book as a  game changer  that requires more sophisticated models.

International accounting rules coming into force next year, called IFRS 9, will require banks to set aside money in advance for potential loan losses. They put added emphasis on models, as do the stress tests banks go through all over the world to prove they can withstand crises. The US stress test, in particular, puts additional emphasis on modelling.

 The regulatory pressure on modelling comes from a number of different directions. Capital requirements isn t the only one,  says Tom Huertas, a partner at EY, the professional services firm, and a former alternate chair of the European Banking Authority.

Mr Bluhm says banks will continue to invest heavily in operational risk management, ploughing resources into automation, new technology, fraud prevention and artificial intelligence in a bid to reduce the risk of something going wrong with their operations.

Investors have been horrified by the soaring cost of risk management, which has left some wondering whether banks will be able to produce the return on equity they expect. Mr Huertas says he expects banks to make efficiencies as they invest further. He also suggests lenders may try to cut costs by using in-house staff to do work on risk management that is currently carried out for them by expensive consultants.

THE MONDAY INTERVIEW
France's tech champion turns venture capitalist; The Monday Interview: Fleur Pellerin, Chair, Korelya Capital

By Harriet Agnew 
1,105 words
13 March 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
26
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The former minister laid the foundations for a digital economy. Now she is putting them to the test, writes Harriet Agnew

When Fleur Pellerin set up the venture capital firm Korelya last year, the first contact the former French Socialist minister had with the government was a letter from the revenue outlining the taxes her business would pay.

"It's not a very friendly way to start," laughs 43-year-old Ms Pellerin, who as a government minister from 2012 to 2016 spearheaded a push to make Paris a "start-up republic". She says: "The administration still could do better to make the relations with entrepreneurs a bit smoother."

We are in Korelya's Paris offices, in an area becoming known as Silicon Sentier as start-ups and investors move in. This is due in no small part to Ms Pellerin's efforts to nurture tech during two years as minister for small and medium-sized enterprises, innovation and the digital economy.

Now her career has changed direction. Last year she raised  100m from Naver, the South Korean internet services company, to invest in French and European start-ups working in "deep tech" - artificial intelligence, big data and machine learning.

So far Korelya has invested alongside US rapper Jay Z in Devialet, a French sound technology business founded in 2007, earning her a seat on the board. Last month it invested an undisclosed amount in the latest fund from Atomico, the venture capital firm run by Niklas Zennstrm, co-founder of Skype.

For Ms Pellerin, Korelya marks a return to her roots. She was born in Seoul, South Korea, but abandoned on the streets when she was days old. Ms Pellerin was eventually adopted by French parents and had a modest upbringing in the suburbs of Paris. Strong academic performance helped her to attend prestigious French schools, including cole nationale d'administration, training ground for the French technocratic elite - for her, proof that "France is still a country where you can achieve social mobility".

After nearly 15 years as a civil servant and working on three Socialist presidential campaigns, Ms Pellerin was appointed a minister by Franois Hollande when he was elected president in 2012. She says: "It was very difficult in Europe to grow great companies that would compete with the big American or Chinese players . . . There was a need for an ecosystem."

She focused on start-ups and her plan had three parts: education, funding and branding. She encouraged France's education system to develop expertise in areas such as big data and coding. Then there was funding. French - and European - start-ups suffered from a lack of capital to help companies scale up. The government created a public investment bank called Bpifrance in 2011, which has since invested more than  1.6bn in France's tech industry.

Capitals across Europe were competing for tech business and Paris was not on the map, says Ms Pellerin. Inspired by efforts such as Tech City in London and Silicon Allee in Berlin, she helped brand the project French Tech.

Collective efforts from Ms Pellerin and others, including telecoms entrepreneur Xavier Niel (whose  250m Station F incubator in Paris will house 1,000 start-ups), have paid off. Momentum is growing in French tech, which last year accounted for a fifth of all the money raised by European start-ups, equal to Germany and behind only the UK, according to an EY report.

For Ms Pellerin, Korelya came about somewhat "unexpectedly" after she was ousted from government. She had been discussing the "digital revolution" with Lee Hae-jin, Naver's chairman , "a sort of Steve Jobs in Korea". On hearing that Naver wanted to increase its exposure to Europe, she proposed to work with it on a venture capital firm.

Ms Pellerin's demeanour is calm, efficient and friendly. She acknowledges she has no investment experience, but can bring an understanding of how the French government works. And Korelya's access to the Asian market through Naver appeals to companies seeking investment.

She talks about the agility of running a start-up and the relative ease of motivating a small team compared with the bureaucracy of government.

Her four years in government ended in 2016, when Ms Pellerin lost her job in a reshuffle. By then she was culture minister. But she provoked outrage in France when she failed in an interview to name a book by French novelist Patrick Modiano after he won the 2014 Nobel Prize for literature. She admitted she had not read a book for two years.

Now, less than two months from France's presidential election, the Socialist party is facing crisis. Left-winger Benot Hamon beat former prime minister Manuel Valls in the presidential nomination, marking a shift to the left and exposing divisions within the party. Some pro-business Socialist MPs and supporters have backed centrist Emmanuel Macron, who is standing as an independent candidate.

The Socialist party "is in a bad state", says Ms Pellerin, because of "a lack of work on the clarification of our ideology" on globalisation, the digital revolution and labour market reform. Mr Hamon campaigned on a return to leftwing policies such as a monthly universal income, which would be partly financed by a tax on robots. Ms Pellerin is against such a tax. But she agrees that the consequences of automation on the workforce is a problem.

She says she will not be galvanising pro-business Socialists to support Mr Macron. Ms Pellerin believes his proposed economic reforms could not be implemented without strong parliamentary support, "which he will certainly lack".

She adds: "A lot of people have difficulties with Macron because they don't perceive him as a socialist. His programme is the same as [centre-right candidate Franois] Fillon's, almost.

"This is the first time in my life, six weeks before the first round of elections, that I don't know for whom I'm going to vote. It's a bit sad."

Second opinion

The prime minister

Bernard Cazeneuve, France's prime minister, met Ms Pellerin when the pair worked on Franois Hollande's presidential campaign, and they became friends. He says: "French tech was invented by Fleur. She tried to involve French business people in it on their trips to the US. We had to explain what we were doing, as people thought we weren't business-friendly.

"She has a strong will and strength due to her intellect and ability to bring people together," he adds.

Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Government-bond prices on both sides of the Atlantic dropped again Thursday amid signs that the European Central Bank won't pursue additional stimulus measures.

As expected, the ECB on Thursday left its key interest rates and plans for bond purchases unchanged. But at a news conference following the central bank's policy meeting, ECB President Mario Draghi said the risk of deflation in the eurozone had diminished, removing the urgency for taking further actions.

Those comments sparked an especially large selloff among German bonds, with the yield on the 10-year German bond rising to 0.429% from 0.376% Wednesday. The yield on the benchmark 10-year U.S. Treasury note, meanwhile, climbed to 2.596% from 2.552% Wednesday.

Yields rise when bond prices fall.

Before Mr. Draghi's remarks, U.S. government bonds had already had a tough few days, extending a selloff that began last week when Federal Reserve officials strongly signaled plans to raise interest rates at their March 14-15 policy meeting.

The yield on the two-year Treasury note, which is especially sensitive to the Fed's policy outlook, rose to 1.370% on Thursday from 1.354% Wednesday. That was its highest close since June 8, 2009.

One catalyst for higher yields was a report Wednesday from payroll processor Automatic Data Processing Inc. and forecasting firm Moody's Analytics that showed the U.S. private sector added 298,000 jobs last month, the most since April 2014.

That report is seen as a preview of the more closely watched jobs report from the Labor Department, which was to be released Friday.

With investors now expecting tighter monetary policy in the U.S. and Europe, "the momentum is definitely there" for bond yields to keep rising, said Stanley Sun, interest-rates strategist at Nomura Securities International in New York.

At the same time, if the Fed signals next week that it still expects three interest-rate increases this year, that could "calm down the market a little bit," he added.

Markets
Government Bond Yields Rise After ECB's Draghi Signals No Extra Stimulus; Yields have been climbing steadily after Federal Reserve officials signaled plans to raise rates

By Sam Goldfarb 
791 words
9 March 2017
20:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. government bonds sold off again Thursday amid further signs that major central banks could be close to turning the page on an era of ultraloose monetary policies.

The yield on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note settled at 2.596%, its highest close since Dec. 16, compared with 2.552% Wednesday.

Yields, which rise when bond prices fall, have been climbing steadily over the past week after Federal Reserve officials "https://www.wsj.com/articles/yellen-signals-likely-rate-increase-at-march-fed-meeting-1488564006" that they plan to raise interest rates at their March 14-15 policy meeting.

On Thursday, attention shifted to the European Central Bank, which "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ecb-leaves-rates-borrowing-costs-unchanged-1489064015" and made no changes to its bond-buying program at the conclusion of its latest policy meeting. That decision initially caused a modest rally in government bonds, which reversed when ECB President Mario Draghi made several comments at a press conference that suggested the central bank was becoming more optimistic about the eurozone economy and less inclined to provide additional stimulus.

In recent years, government bond yields have been held at historically low levels in large part because central banks have gone to unprecedented lengths to bolster moribund economies by keeping interest rates at or below zero and pursuing large-scale bond purchases.

Though the Fed ended its bond-purchase program in 2014, it raised interest rates only once in each of the past two years while its counterparts in Europe and Japan stepped up their stimulus programs, helping to keep a lid on bond yields globally.

Even with no official change to its key policies, just the suggestion that the ECB was shifting its outlook caused a significant reaction in the bond market. The yield on the 10-year German bond closed at 0.429%, according to Tradeweb, compared with 0.376% Wednesday.

This is hardly the first time that government bonds have sold off in recent years. Episodes such as the "taper tantrum" of 2013 were followed by large bond rallies as expected economic growth and interest-rate increases failed to materialize.

More recently, the yield on the 10-year Treasury note reached 2.6% in the aftermath of President Donald Trump's electoral win. Investors at the time were betting that Mr. Trump's campaign promises of large tax cuts and infrastructure spending could become law and boost growth and inflation.

Since then, enthusiasm for the so-called "Trump trade" has dissipated somewhat among bond investors but may have been replaced by something more substantial, with strong economic data potentially giving Fed officials reason to quicken the pace of their interest-rate increases.

In just the latest in a run of solid data, a report Wednesday from payroll processor Automatic Data Processing Inc. and forecasting firm Moody's Analytics showed the U.S. private sector "https://www.wsj.com/articles/private-sector-added-more-jobs-than-expected-in-february-1488980927", the most since April 2014. That report is seen as a preview of the more closely watched jobs report from the Labor Department that will be released Friday.

Some investors and analysts have speculated that the bond selloff could pick up additional momentum if yields cross key thresholds, such as 2.6% for the 10-year note.

At the same time, yields may have climbed high enough already that the bar has been raised for upcoming events, such as the jobs report and next week's Fed meeting.

With investors now expecting tighter monetary policy in the U.S. and Europe, "the momentum is definitely there" for bond yields to keep rising, said Stanley Sun, interest-rates strategist at Nomura Securities International in New York. But if the Fed signals next week that it still expects just three interest-rate increases this year that could "calm the market a little bit," he added.

When the bond market first came under pressure at the end of last year, one concern was that higher yields on their own could ultimately lead to lower yields by causing interest rates to rise more broadly and constraining economic activity.

So far, there has been little sign of that happening. Rather than falling along with Treasurys, prices on riskier corporate debt have climbed in recent months, ensuring low borrowing costs for even heavily indebted companies.

At some point, though, there probably is a limit to how high yields can rise in the current economic environment, with it difficult to imagine a 10-year yield much above 3.25%, said Nick Maroutsos, a fixed-income portfolio manager of Janus Capital Group.

Typically, the market "overshoots in either direction and then it readjusts," he added.

Hedge funds adopt novel methods to hunt down new tech talent

Robin Wigglesworth 
587 words
9 March 2017
04:25
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Graphic novels, coding competitions, mammoth pay packets and even entire university research centres are just some of the tactics hedge funds are turning to as they seek to win an intensifying war for technology talent.

So-called quantitative hedge funds and investment banks have long fought with Silicon Valley for the brightest programmers and data scientists, but traditional asset managers are now throwing themselves into the fray, in a big bet that the future of investing is machine intelligence and Big Data, not human nous and quarterly corporate results.

 The talent war is unprecedented,  said Yin Luo, head of quantitative research at Wolfe Research and a former top strategist at Deutsche Bank.  It s nearly impossible to find people with experience in both computer science and finance. So it s all about raw talent. 

To unearth this raw talent, investment groups are trying unorthodox approaches such as coding competitions and freestyle data challenges to unearth hidden gems outside the usual elite universities.

Two Sigma, one of the biggest quant hedge funds, is running a "https://www.kaggle.com/c/two-sigma-financial-modeling", an online platform popular with data scientists, where contestants will have three months to code a trading algorithm based on four gigabytes of financial data provided by the US hedge fund. The winner will soon pocket $100,000.

Ostensibly the aim of the contest is to raise awareness and interest in finance, but the hedge fund also hopes to find some potential hires among Kaggle s 730,000 user base from across the world.

Instead of a traditional but staid leaflet, potential hires at Two Sigma are given a graphic novel that explains the hedge fund s approach and benefits, such as a  Monte Carlo Hill Climbing Lunch , where a Monte Carlo algorithm once a week selects four  Sigmas  to invite. If at least three accept, the lunch is arranged.  As if there were a better way to pick people to eat with,  the graphic novel says.

 While attracting talent is never easy, we find that our culture and people are a differentiator as we speak with potential candidates,  said Scott Grabarski, head of engineering human resources at Two Sigma.

The Kaggle competition follows in the footsteps of WorldQuant, a hedge fund that manages $4.5bn for Izzy Englander s Millennium Management. It runs a   "https://websim.worldquantchallenge.com/en/cms/wqc/home/"  designed to give  curious and strategic-minded individuals the opportunity to learn about quantitative finance, compete with global participants and potentially earn a research consultant position  at the hedge fund.

Underscoring the battle for talent as new players enter the space, WorldQuant late last year sued a former "https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2016-12-22/data-scientists-making-millions-vie-with-fund-managers-over-pay"for breach of contract when he left to join Dan Loeb s Third Point, alleging that he had been promised a tenfold increase in his salary to $2m. The case was quickly settled out of court.

Several UK hedge funds have led the way in partnering with universities to access the talent pipeline directly. For example, Man Group has financed a centre for  machine learning    a field of artificial intelligence   at Oxford university, and last year Cambridge university opened the Cantab Capital Institute for Mathematics of Information, funded by Cantab Capital.

For smaller firms the challenges in finding good staff can be particularly intense.  The talent is the hardest part. A lot of my time is simply spent cultivating people,  said Scott Borgerson, the head of CargoMetrics, a Boston-based quant hedge fund.  World-class people are very sought-out.

ANALYSIS
Lessons from the 'quant quake'; FT BIG READ: INVESTMENT

By Robin Wigglesworth 
2,255 words
9 March 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
30
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Nearly a decade after the sector's near collapse humbled some of Goldman Sachs' leading financial scientists, computer-driven quantitative investing is once again the hottest trend in finance. Is it safe yet? By Robin Wigglesworth

It was a typical New York summer day, the kind where arriving at Goldman Sachs' perfectly air-conditioned offices in downtown Manhattan was a blissful release from the humid weather outside. But for Gary Chropuvka it proved to be one of the worst days of his life.

Mr Chropuvka worked at Goldman's money management arm, specifically at Quantitative Investment Strategies, a division staffed by mathematicians, computer scientists and physicists. Even at Goldman, the QIS employees were considered intellectual superstars. Their prowess at decoding the signals of financial markets meant the unit managed $165bn at its peak - more than any hedge fund group .

But on August 6 2007, everything unravelled. As soon as US markets started trading, the previously wildly successful automated investment algorithms coded by the QIS brainiacs went horribly awry, and losses mounted at a frightening pace.

What became known as the "quant quake" subsided in a week and was largely contained within the computer-powered investment industry. It was soon overshadowed by the global financial crisis. But it scarred a generation of financial scientists on Wall Street. Even Renaissance Technologies, the legendary hedge fund co-founded by cold war codebreaker James Simons , suffered painful losses, and it nearly obliterated Goldman's QIS.

"All this worked academically, and for a long time it worked in practice, and then all of a sudden you have this horrible event," Mr Chropuvka says. "It was the most humbling experience of our lives."

Nearly a decade later, quantitative investing is once again the hottest trend in finance. Computer-driven hedge funds have just notched up their eighth straight year of client inflows, doubling their assets from 2009 to $918bn, according to Hedge Fund Research. Even this understates the interest, as many traditional hedge funds and big mutual fund managers are all trying to blend more quantitative techniques with their traditional approaches.

QIS is emblematic of the quant renaissance . In 2011 Goldman Sachs put its top computer wizard, Armen Avanessians, in charge of the division. He has helped turn round its fortunes. The arm's assets under management reached a nadir of $38bn in 2012, but it now manages $91.8bn - still below the unit's pre-crisis peak.

"The first thing I did was to fly to our biggest clients and apologise," he says. "All bad things involve crowding and leverage, and the quant crisis was no different. But the core idea that computers can do a lot of this better than humans was right . . . I feel that we're just at the early stages of this quant revolution, and that gets me excited."

The explosive growth of algorithmic investing - whether high-frequency traders, next-generation exchange traded funds or artificial intelligence-powered hedge funds - has transformed the markets. Some analysts fear that another 2007-style meltdown would be more severe due to the proliferation of quant strategies.

"It's the biggest worry I have," says Richard Bookstaber, a former risk manager at Morgan Stanley and Moore Capital, now a research principal at the US Treasury's Office of Financial Research. "What is going on now is not just the growth of quant hedge funds, like before the crisis. Now it's system-wide across the investment world. In 2007 it was localised because no one else was pursuing these strategies. Now everyone is."

Persistent signals

Roughly a dozen analysts and fund managers shuffle into an airy corner office at Goldman's New York headquarters to hear the co-heads of research at QIS's Equity Alpha Strategies team, Dennis Walsh and Takashi Suwabe, present some of their data crunching.

Mr Walsh outlines how they can profit from even the most common of corporate events: quarterly earnings calls. Using an AI technique called "natural language processing", Goldman's algorithms can systematically look for verbal cues from analysts on the call that might indicate whether they were pleasantly or unpleasantly surprised at the results - and therefore upgrade their outlook in response.

"There's a tendency towards praise to keep in management's good books, but only marginally. If 20 out of 30 analysts say 'great quarter' then it probably was," Mr Walsh says. It may seem sketchy, but success for quants depends on detecting small but persistent signals that they can feed into trading algorithms.

QIS has been radically restructured since its near-death experience in 2007, and focuses on three main areas. Its biggest part is the $35bn "tax-efficient strategies" unit which structures investment products for wealthy Goldman clients. But QIS executives say the main priorities are its $31bn "smart beta" ETF business , which invests in specific market factors or characteristics, the $16bn Equity Alpha arm and a small but promising $3.2bn "alternative risk premia" business, where financial engineers try to deconstruct and cheaply replicate hedge fund returns.

The future of the business will be powered by data. "The amount of data out there is awesome," says Mr Chropuvka, one of two Goldman partners at QIS. "Figuring out the linkages between companies has become more complicated, and quants like using computers to help figure these things out. We have for decades played the breadth game, but with big data we can play the depth game. We can do it more comprehensively, and in real time."

Another signal explored by Goldman's quantitative analysts is something they term "geographic momentum", based on how companies in one corner of the planet can often offer clues to how others in the same region are performing.

QIS has crunched the data of its 14,800 stocks across 1m locations, using "machine learning" to find and quantify hidden relationships and linkages that might often cross state, regional or even country borders, and sliced the world into 200 distinct "economic clusters" where companies tend to move in tandem. A French bank with a big loan book in eastern Europe might offer clues to how Polish contractors are doing, or Texan developers might indicate the health of Mexican cement sales.

Leveraged complexity

The investment industry's enthusiasm for the potential of big data, superfast computers and AI is palpable - and not just among quant hedge funds. State Street, a $2.4tn asset manager, believes this is the "next evolutionary step" for the sector. BlackRock, the world's biggest fund manager, has said it "represents a watershed moment in the history of investment management".

The enthusiasm mostly reflects a view of where markets and investing are heading. Aside from a few elite firms, even the industry's more sophisticated players can struggle for consistent returns. HFR's quant hedge fund index has been far less volatile than the broader industry universe, but gained only 1.7 per cent last year.

Yin Luo, head of quantitative research at Wolfe Research, says the pre-crisis era was the "golden age" for the industry, when "almost everything worked, even if it wasn't fancy". Now, it is much harder, with many common signals and strategies copied into oblivion.

Nor is hiring a lot of smart data scientists a silver bullet. BlackRock has poured money into its Scientific Active Equity division, but almost two-thirds of its quant funds underperformed their benchmarks last year, according to a presentation acquired by Bloomberg. Jeff Shen, the unit's co-head, attributes the poor performance to the turbulent start to 2016. But it still highlights that quantitative investing can be tricky.

The head of one big quant hedge fund compares the phenomenon to Kodak, the camera company that saw the rise of digital photography coming but was still unable to refocus the business, and eventually went bankrupt. "Even if you recognise something is changing, it's not easy to revamp the business," he says.

Nonetheless, the quant revolution is transforming investment. The convoluted but broadly explicable financial markets of previous generations have evolved into an electronic jungle of unfathomable complexity.

Understanding how markets function increasingly requires knowledge of a Greek-based alphabet soup of concepts and phenomena - such as alpha generation, smart beta, gamma scalping, delta hedging and option theta. Their speed has accelerated markedly, and the number of market anomalies, odd trading patterns and mysterious "flash crashes" have increased in tandem with the ascent of algorithms.

Yet one of the biggest fears is that the money pouring into quantitative investing could be laying the seeds of a bigger re-run of the 2007 quant quake.

The precise trigger for the quake might never be known, but analysts generally agree the pot was brought to a boil by too many big investors unknowingly making the same trades while juicing up their returns with leverage. When the financial crisis began, one of the bigger players was likely to have been hit by subprime mortgage losses, forcing it to sell its more liquid but highly leveraged equity portfolio to satisfy investor withdrawals. Losses hammered participants with similar trades and triggered fresh rounds of liquidation.

Goldman's QIS division is sometimes fingered as one of the potential culprits in triggering the quant quake - specifically its once-imperious Global Alpha fund that was closed in 2011 - something its executives vehemently deny. QIS was simply one of the biggest victims because several of its funds had used "an unbelievable amount of leverage", Mr Chropuvka says.

'We focus on what can go wrong'

The kind of leverage used in 2007 has largely been banished from the financial system. President Donald Trump's planned regulatory rollback might shake things up, but much of the reduction is attributed to heightened caution after the crisis. Moreover, while billions of dollars have been poured into quantitative strategies in recent years, their variety has increased significantly.

Indeed, the rise of big data could lead to a new golden era for the industry, Mr Shen says. "The world is more complex and diverse now. That makes it harder [to perform], but it makes crowding less of a risk."

QIS is a case in point. While its quants might have tapped into only a dozen or so signals in 2007, they now use more than 250 with much less leverage. That means the dangers of crowded strategies that proved so toxic nearly a decade ago should be reduced.

While Mr Avanessians is "always paranoid that I'm missing something", he says Goldman's quants have learnt the lessons of 2007. "We now have a maniacal focus on what can go wrong, rather than what can go right."

Not everyone is convinced. Mr Bookstaber points out that while leverage is more limited, the swelling "smart beta" industry is a way that another quant quake could hit the masses. With quants becoming more dominant, fears of another electronically generated market meltdown will persist.

"We're caught in a tug of war between Moore's Law and Murphy's Law," says Andrew Lo, finance professor at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, referring to Gordon Moore's prediction that computing power would double nearly every year, and the adage that anything that can go wrong, will go wrong. "Our technology has outgrown our ability to manage it, and the financial world is increasing in complexity and fragility."

Alt-risks

Quants bid to beat hedge funds at their own game

One of the smallest but potentially most intriguing units at Goldman Sachs' Quantitative Investment Services is arguably its "alternative risk premia" unit, where a bunch of financial scientists attempt to reverse-engineer and then replicate the secret sauce of hedge funds.

Over the years, quantitative analysts have discovered that a lot of the market-beating "alpha" returns produced by supposedly skilled fund managers can often be attributed to specific factors and market characteristics, such as momentum or cheap valuations. These factors are now often packaged into exchange traded funds as "smart beta".

But recently, quants say they have even been able to deconstruct and rebuild many of the "risk premia" components of sophisticated hedge fund strategies - such as arbitraging mergers and acquisitions, or placing bets on global bond and currency movements - and sell them at a fraction of the cost.

In April 2012 Goldman Sachs transferred about 20 of its financial engineers from the investment bank's securities side to QIS, where they set up an alternative risk premia division to decipher and duplicate an array of hedge fund products for institutional clients.

So far it has only been a modest success. At the end of last year, the unit's assets under management stood at $3.2bn. But Goldman executives have pinpointed it as one of its three main priorities, convinced that it can become a money-spinning factory for mass production of cheap hedge fund returns for its clients.

Nonetheless, some quants - let alone many hedge fund managers - are sceptical, arguing that a lot of the supposed success of hedge fund replication is based mostly on "backtests", running the models against historical data. And a lot of strategies that work when tested on backward-looking data can fizzle out when confronted with moving markets.

Speed read

Heavy losses Groups such as Goldman Sachs' quantitative division suffered a rout in the August 2007 'quant quake'

Crowding fears The post-crisis growth of quantitative investment has stirred fears over another market storm

Busy signals The industry has grown in complexity, but now analyses many more factors to generate returns
